SECONDING SPEECH BY
ASSEMBLYMAN COBB
Assemblyman VT. H. Cobb of San Fran
cisco was at once recognized by the chair
and followed Valentine In an eloquent sec
onding speech. He spoke as the repre
sentative of those who had so long stood
by Colonel Burns, and his peroration wort
him round after round of applause. Mr.
Cobb had to say:
Many grave measures are pressing forward
for Congressional consideration at this time.
The reciprocity treaties with foreign countries,
the Storage of fiood waters, tha irrigation and
reclamation of desert lands, the Pacific cable,
the Nicaragua canal and the Philippine Islands
all require earnest attention at once. Never
before have so many great questions of vital
Interest to the Pacific Coast arisen at the same
time, and California is more espec^Uly Inter
ested fti all these issues than any other State.
Our commercial and Industrial interests are
rapidly forcing us into broader fields of activity
and we cannot afford to sit supinely by and
see the rich treasures of trade and commerce
slip from our grasp If proper legislation can
secure them to us forever. Our enemies arÂ»
aSake to the opportunity and will checkmate
us If they can. Public as well as party In
terest now demand that our personal prefer
ences give way to the voice of the majority
that a Senator may be elected without delay.
We have Â¦ therefore assembled to complete tha
work we let t undone at our regular session
and to elect a United State* Senator. The
iinportar.ee of his dalle* requires that he bo
a man of distinguished ability. He need not
necessarily be an orator, but he must be a
thinker, able to think with the shrewdest men
of the nation. Incompetency would be as
dangerous as the basest treachery. Better a
vacant chair than to fill it with either a
blunderer or a traitor. He must be upright
and honorable, of known fidelity to the State
and nation, and must possess the win power
and energy of Hercules to accomplish the
work exoected of him at this time. The time
has come lor us to unite and place the toga
upon the shoulders of an. honest, abie. ener
getic and courageous man! who will make an
aggressive fight on behalf of the interests
peculiar to this coast and assist us to win a>
victory in our struggle for commercial and in
dustrial supremacy.
The gentleman whose name has Just been
placed before you possesses every requisite of
this important office to a marked degree. - It*
needs no one to vouch for him. His character
Is plainly written in. his face. Culture and re
finement fit him like a garment. While ne has
all the advantages which education and stud?
can give, his steady eye shows a wisdom
broader and deeper than the learning taught
In schools and marks him as a man eminently
fitted and qualified to fill this resQonsible po
sition with credit to himself end honor to thÂ»
State and nation. And his selection at thla
time is peculiarly appropriate. In the bitter
party strife Just ended no wound Is attributable
to his hand. Time with its soothing touch Is
not needed to smooth down feelings ruffled by
his success. The State and party will welcome
his election with unanimous approval. All Â¦of
us can unite In his support without disloyalty
to our particular candidate. I still assert my
confidence in the character, ability. Integrity
and fitness of Colonel Burns for the office you
have denied him. but on behalf of his friends I
am authorised to say ttat we accept Mr. Bard
as the most satisfactory substitute you could
have chosen for us. While he la your candi
date he will be our Senator.
California Is in its infancy. Its resources ars>
undeveloped and unknown. Its marvelous in
crease In public and private wealth in tha
past sinks into insignificance before the start
ling possibilities of the future. Nature has
scattered her treasures with a lavish hars.t
along the shores of the mighty Pacific, and w<Â»
have faith to believe that our hardy pioneers
planted here the seeds of & mlgthy empire,
which in the fullness of, time will develop a
grander ctvllliatlon than any the world has
e\er known. The man with the hoe. the man
with the pen and the man with the sword have
already started the wheels of progress turning,
and whether the future shall bring new bless
ings to our State or shall scatter the treasures
gathered with so much toll and suffering de
pends upon the men who snap* oar future in
dustrial, educational and political policies.
i Mr Speaker, and members of the Assembly,
with a full appreciation of th# duties and re
sponsibilities of the office and with full con
fidence In the character and ability of the man.
I take pleasure in seconding the. nomination
as a candidate for United States Senator th*
Hon. Thomas K. Bard.

rive in San Francisco Saturday

morning.

spect the .frrtifi cations it Lime
Point and Fort Point. The par

ator.

iticians loitering and spying
around for Senatorial develop

urday night next. To-mor
row at noon Senator Flint will

VALENTINE ELOQUENTLY
NOMINATES BARD
Valentine, in placing the Venturan in
nomination, said:
Mr. Speaker arid gentlemen of the Assembly:
During the whole of the last session of the
Legislature several distinguished gentlemen.
citizens of the State of California, were sup
ported loyally throughout the whole session by
their supporters In Joint convention, voting for
them as candidates for the United States Sen
ate. That they were entitled to the confidence
not only of their supporters, but of the people
of the State of California is manifest,
and it -Is fortunate for this great nation of
ours, magnificent as she is with all her 're
sources, to have so many who can so well
and ably represent her in the higher branch
of the legislative halls in Washington. The
Governor of the State, seeing the necessity
of full representation in. the Congressional
halls, has placed as foremost in his proclama
tion calling us together In this extraordinary
session the election of a United States Senator,
and I think none of you will deny that that
Is the question paramount in the State of Cali
fornia to-day.
Situated on this western coast of the coun
try we will be first to receive the advantage
of the . Oriental trade, where we .will be the
first "to receive the advantage of the expansion
taking in. thus, as we hare the Islands of
the Orient ar.d over which Old Glory will
wave forever, taking in as we have the islands
midway In the Pacificâ the Hawaiian Islands
all of these benefits will first come to the
door of California. We will first receive the
advantage of these acquisitions, and it Is
proper and right that we should have full
representation In both branches of Congress
so that California may derive the full benefit
of these advantages.
When we come to. the question of who shall
be the Senator to represent us and act as
the colleague of the Hon. George C. Perkins
we have been deliberating over that question
for some time. We have a man. coming not
particularly from the south, not from the
north, not from the east or the west, but from
the whole State of California; one who Is
broad ' enough, who Is great enough In every
capacity to -fully, represent the great State of
pleasure in saying It. Special stress was
made of the fact that Mr. Bard's selec
tion was a case of the office seeking the
man. Attention was also called to the
action of leading Republican candidates
for the office who had cast aside their
personal ambitions In order that the fu
ture of their party in California wquld
not be Jeopardized.
In marked contrast to the enthusiasm
that greeted the name of Thomas R. Bard
was the gloom that spread over the Sen
ate like a pall when the minority pre
sented James D. Phelan as its candidate.
No applause followed his nomination: and
when the roll was called several of the
Democrats refused to vote.
SENATOR ROWELL PLACES
BARD IN NOMINATION
Mr. Bard was placed In nomination by
Senator Rowell, whose address, while
brief, awakened all of the pent-up enthu
siasm of the supporters of the gentleman
from the south, and at the close they
burst forth In round after round of cheer-
Ing. The Senator said:
Mr. President: A little more than one year
ago I had the pleasure of presenting to this
body for its Senatorial suffrage the name of
Thorns s R. Bard. To-day it gives me double
pleasure to again present the same name for
a like suffrage. Then it was the personal ex
pression of long friendship and exalted ad
miration for the man. now It is an expression
of the spontaneous sentiment of this body and
with the spontaneous and hearty approval of
the people of the State of California.
At least for once In ths history of our politics
a great and generally coveted position has
sought the man and not the man the position.
Mr. President, there is little that need be said
in behalf of the man whose name I rtTtr. If
mature years, a high order of Intelligence, a
life's honorable record, a comprehensive knowl
edge of men "and affairs and a thcrougn ap
preciation of and faith In those principles of
government which we believe have been con
ducive to the welfare of our country are â¢rual
lflrations then this body has found the man
for United States Senator. But, Mr. Presi
dent, there is more in this election of a Sena
tor than merely the selection of a well qual
ified public servant or the expression of ad
miration for or good will toward an honorable
citizen. The electwn of Thomas R. Bard will
mean an emphatic expression of a rÂ»pidly
growing sentiment throughout our land that
politics In this country, must and shall l>e con
ducted on higher planes. I bespeak for Mr.
Bard, if elected, an honorable standing among
the Senators of the nation, and 1 promise fcr
him that he. will represent California and all
her interests at the national capital with
credit to himself and to the honor and to the
benefit of the State.

MENTO, Feb. 6.â The last trace of what
ever ' heartburnings ' may have resulted

to-night when the contending factions and
the citizens of Sacramento gave a recep
tion in honor of California's new Senator,
The parlor floor of the Golden Eagle was
"the scene of the festivities. : Potted plants,
palms and cut flowers were used in. pro
fusion to decorate the t"eceptlon hall, cor-
Iridors and the rooms occupied by Senator
Bard "as his headquarters.^, f v V
The reception began about 9 o'clock and
lasted until after midnlghj. Those who
had been the most bitter opponents of the
Venturan were the most hearty and sin
cere in their congratulations and expres
sions of fealty. The wives and daughters
of many of the legislators and some of
the. most prominent society ladles of the
capital city formed one of the most at
tractive features of the reception.
Senator \Bard, the guest of honor, was
the 'center around which all revolved. The
smiles with which his countenance beamed
told plainly that he appreciated the cor
diality of the reception and saw much for
'congratulation in the recementlng of Re
publican ties. Mrs. Thomas Flint, wife
of Senator Flint, and Mrs. Gray assisted
Senator; Bard in receiving the guests.
'; The health and success 'of the Senator
were wished from every Up, while empha
sis was given to the expressions of good
w.ill by liberal bumpers of sparkling wine.
Supper was prepared by Mr. Potter of the
Van Nuys Hotel, Los Angeles.
his counsel widely sought; In his political life
he has the ability, the integrity, the wisdom
and the breadth to do honor to this great State

tutions and her future will be safe in his
hands. ... ',;?.Â«

also spoke from the standpoint of an ex

Mr. Speaker and Gentlemen of the Assembly:
For nearly one hundred days have we labored
with this proposition trying to nominate a can
didate for United States Senator who shall
succeed the Hon. Stephen M. White. For near
ly one. hundred days have we been divided' in
faction,; and to-day, as representatives, we are
united here and hope to see go out from this
assemblage the words to the people of this
State, that we have united upon a man in
whom we have the utmost confidence. I do not'
desire to refer to his ability, honesty and in
tegrity; I take it, gentlemen, that the mere
fact that his nomination upon this floor Is due
evidence to every one of . these virtues.
It has been my duty to follow In the foot
steps of one whom I have ever found true to
the Republican party and the State. My loy
alty to him la. as great as It has ever been,
but the majority of the Republicans in this
House have agreed to nominate another candi
date and that candidate to-day is my candi
date. - Wo are told by him to give our honor,
our best efforts to the election of Thomas R.
Bard and . we are here to-day to carry out
thÂ« words of him whom, we supported so honor
ably last -session."
f Gentlemen of California, we are anxious that
wo have our full share In the Senators of the
United Statea and we realize that to-day we
are to perform a great blessing upon the State
of California when we say to her that we have
selected a man for this position and, gentle
men, it gives me great pleasure at this time
to second the nomination of Thomas R. Bard.

United States Cannot Fortify
the Waterway.
WASHINGTON; Feb. 6.-Secretary Hay
Is determined that the State Department
f-he!l observe with the most scrupulous
fidelity whatever obligation may rest "upon
it toward Nicaragua and Costa Rica in
tho matter of the construction of the
N'icarag-uan canal. It has not been possi
ble to move In that matter pending the
removal â¢of the obstacle in the way of
canal building presented by the Clayton-
Bulwer treaty. There has not been the
slightest disposition to ignore the Gov
ernments of our sister republics in the
matter, but, in the view of the depart
ment, it would rot have" been dignified
or safe to make overtures to them before
the United States had acquired the power
to deal with a free hand with the canal
problem bo far as Great Britain was co:i
cerned. Now, however, the department
v.-ill address itself to the Governments cf
Nicaragua and Costa Rica looking to the
acquirement of whatever privileges are
deemed essential to the construction of
the canal and its operation.
About the same time, or. rather, as coon
as> the Senate shall have ratified the
treaty and the ratifications shall have
been exchanged, the department will ad
dress identical notes to the great mari
time powers of the whole world to secure
their adhesion to .the project for the per
petual neutrality of the canal.
The following is the text of the new
Treaty negotiated in relation to the con
etruction of the Nicaraguan canal:
The United Ptates of America, and her Ma
jesty, the Queen of the United Kingdom of
<ireat I2rita.in and Ireland Â«nd Empress of In
dia, being desirous to facilitate the construction
ct a ship canal lo connect the Atlantic and
Pacific oceans and to that end remove any ob
jection which may arise out of the convention
of April IS, ISM, commonly called the Clayton-
Bulwer treaty, to the construction of such
<-anal undf-r the ausplr-es of the Government of
the United .States, without Impairing "the gen
*jtl principle" of neutralization established in
article VIII of that convention have for that
jiurpose appointed as their plenlpotentarles
âby the President of the United States
John Hay. and hy her Majesty, the Queen
of Great Britain Â«nd Ireland and Em-
X>reÂ«s of India. the Right Hon. Lord
Pauncefote, G. C. 8., G. C. M. G., her
Majesty's Embassador . extraordinary and
plenipotentiary to the United States., who, hav
ing communk-ated to each other their full
jwiwers, which were found to be In due and
proper form, have agreed upon the following
Articles:
Article Iâ lt Is agreed that the canal may rxÂ»
c-onstracted under the sufplces of the Govern
ment of the United States, either directly at
Its own cost or by gift or loan of. the money
to Individuals or _by corporations or through
Fubscrlptlon to or* purchases of stocks, or
shares, axid that, subject to the provisions of
the present convention, the raid Government
Fhall have and enjoy the rights incident to such
â¢â¢onstruction Ef well as the exclusive right of
providing for the regulation and management
Â«pf thÂ» canal.
Articie 11â The high contracting parties, de
ririnr to preserve and maintain the "general
principle" of neutralization established in
article VIII of ,the Clayton-Bulwer conven
tion, adopt as the b&cJ* of such neutralization
the following rule*, substantially as embodied
In the convention between Great Britain and
certain other powers signed at Constantinople
or. Octobei 1?, ISSS,. for the free navigation of
the Suez maritime canal; lhat is to say:
. 1. The canal shall be free and open in time
X>f war as in timp of peace to the vessels of
O'lnmerce and of war Â»t all nations on terms
of equity, so that there shall be no discrim
ination against any nation or its citizens or
sublets in respect of the conditions or charges
Of traffic or otherwise
. 2. The ranal shall never be blockaded nor
shall any right of war be exercised nor any
act of hostility be commljted within it.
3. Vessels of war of a belligerent shall not
revictual nor take any stores in the canal ex
cept so far as may be atrictly necessary; and
the transit of such vessels through the canal
rhall be effected with the least possible delay
in" accordance with the regulations In force
and with only such intermission as may result
from the necessities of the service. Prizes
shall be in all respects subject to the same
rules as vessels of war of the belligerents.
4. No belligerent shall embark or disembark
troops, munitions of war or warlike materials
in the canal except In case of accidental hind
rance of the transit and in such case the tran
sit shall be resumed with all possible dispatch.
5. The provisions of this article shall apply
to waters adjacent to the canal within three
marine miles of either end. Vessels of war of
a belligerent shall not remain In such waters
longer than twenty-four hours at any one time,
except In cas? of distress, and In such case
thÂ«!l depart as soon as possible, but a Vessel
of war of one belligerent shall not depart with
in twenty-four hours from the departure of
the vessel of war of another belligerent.
6. The plant and establishments, buildings,
all works necessary to construction, mainten
ance and opening of the canal shall be deemed
to be part thereof, for the purposes of this
convention, and in time of war as In time of
peace shall enjoy ample immunity from attack
or injury by belligerents, and from acts cal
culated to impair their usefulness a.Â» part of
the canal.
7. Ko fortifications shall be erected command-
Ing the canal or the waters adjacent. The
United States, however, shall be at liberty to
maintain such military police alone the canal
as may be necessary to protect it against law
lessness and disorder.
Article lllâ The high contracting: parties will
immediately upon the exchange of the ratifica
tions of this convention bring It to the notice of
other rowers and Invite them to adhere, to it.
Article IVâ The pr*Â»em convention shall he
ratified by the President of the United States,
by and with the advice and consent of the
Senate thereof, and by her Britannic Majesty,
and the ratifications *hal! be exchanged â¢ at
Washington or at London within fix months
from the date hereof, or earlier if possihle.
In faith whereof, the respective plenipoten
tiaries have signed this convention, and there
unto affixed their eeal?.

Cincinnati Jurist to Head the Body
That Will Establish Civil Gov
ernment on the Islands.
T T TASHINGTON. Feb. 6.â The
\A / President ! to-day appointed
1/1/ "Judge Â¦ William H. Taft of Cin
â¢ V V clnnati. United States Judge of
* * the Sixth Judicial Circuit, to
be president of the new Philippine Com
mission. Judge Taft's place on the bench
will be filled by the appointment of Judge
Henry F. Severans 6f Grand Rapids.
Mich These announcements were made
at the close of the Cabinet meeting to
day. â¢ â¢ - - .
Judge Taft has been in Washington sev
eral days, presumably coming at the re
quest of the President. This morning he
called at the White House a little before
10 o'clock in company with Judge WilUam
R Day. former' Secretary of State and
later president "of the Paris Peace* Com
mission.. They remained with the Presi
dent until the Cabinet meeting at 11
o'clock and joined him again after the
Cabinet had adjourned.
On leaving the White House he said the
commission of which he had been ap
pointed president would sail from San
Francisco soon after March 15. and its
special mission would be the establish
ment of a civil government for the
islands. Further than that he was not at
liberty to speak. It is understood that the
other members of the commission will be
designated within a very- short time and
that special instructions which will guide
them in the discharge of their duties will
be prepared at as early a day as possible.
The appointment of this commission Is
carrying out the suggestions of the pres
ent Philippine Commission, which will
shortly go*out of existence. Some of the
members of the old commission, it is said,
will be appointed on the new one. ...
The appointment of Judge Taft marks
the first selection for the new Philippine
Commission. There will be five member*
on the commission, and all of them will
be civilians. Schurman. president of the
existing commission, has declined for
business reasons to remain on the com
mission, so Judge Taft will be president
in his place. Mr. Denby and Professor
Worcester have been invited to accept
appointments, but so far have not signi
fied their intentions. Admiral Dewey will
not go back to Manila, and General Otis
will give way, like him, to a civilian.'
Secretary Root said to-day that no in
structions had been framed for the new
commission as yet. but it would be easy
to perceive from the sentiments set out
In the President's message on tne subject
what these instructions would be.
Judge Taft called at the War Depart
ment to-day in company with Judge L>ay.
lat# Secretary of State, and had a long
talk with Secretary Root as to the plans
of the commission.' As slime surprise has
been caused by the willingness of Judge
Taft to surrender a life position of the
dignity and emolument of a United Statea
Circuit Judge to Join the commission, it
may be said that the prevalent impression
is ' that as soon as the commission shall
have succeeded In replacing the existing
military government in the Philippines
with stable civil government Judge Taft
will be named as the first civil Governor
General of the archipelago. It is not
thought that this change will take plaeo
at once, but the commission 1s expected
to move steadily toward that object and
to set up local civil governments as fast
as the Filipinos shall demonstrate their
worthiness.

amalgam of iron and zinc is least sub-
ject to deterioration' from the influence of

W. C. T. U. Proud of Her Majesty.
The temperance- women are rejoicing
oveÂ» the fact that when the Prince of
Monaco, whose Income is derived from
Monte Carlo, desired to pay his respects
to Queen Victoria her Majesty refused to
meet him, thereby evincing her disap
proval of gambling. Another ground for
congratulations to the temperance work
ers Is that her Majesty had a prayer of
fered at the launching of her new yacht,
the ' Victoria and Albert, instead of the
customary breaking of a bottle of wine
over the bow.â New York Tribune. *
by a wave of prosperity. This will have
considerable bearing on the next |Presi
dential election and will very materially
improve the chances for the renomination
of Mr. McJvinley. When times are good
it is not as easy to stir up a hot political
campaign as when they are not. If things
are going along well the people rest con
tented with what they have and are not
so anxious for a change. This makes tho
task of retaining what it has much
easier to the party in power, whatever
party that may be.
"I don't think it will he possible for the
Republicans or any other party to pre
vent the renomlnatlon of Mr. Bryan. As
to the platform on which he will work, I
imagine it will be much the same as the
last one. The silver plank will be of the
same dimensions. If silver was good in
ISS6 I can see no reason why it will not be
just as good, in fact better, in 1900 I
don"t know who will head the ticket with
Mr. Bryan. I will not. lam entirely out
of the race as far as any elective office is
concerned.
"As to steel, iron and ship-building I
have been so long out of the country that
I am not as well posted on these matters
as I would like to be. I can say, how
ever, that the price of both steel and iron
is abnormally high Just now. owing to the
demand for manufacturing and other pur
poses. This demand has been merely the
logical result of the general forward
movement in all lines of business activ
ity. As these two articles are at the bot
tom of all manufacture, their price nat
urally goes up in proportion to demand
created by Increased activity in manufac
turing. These prices will not last through
the year. They have merely been created
by the sudden boom that has followed
the previous depression, and they will
soon sink to something more normal In
its nature I don't mean by this that the
prices will po down to where they were
before the advent of good times.- I mean
that they will get to somewhere nearer
reasonable figures.
"Shlp-bulldlng is rood, but would be bet
ter were it not for the price of steel and
iron, which is so largely used in construc
tion. We require more tonnage and we
have the yards and mechanics to supply
It; but at present wÂ« are waiting for the
cost of material to come within the proper
bounds before going ahead at the rate, we
should like to.
"I look for these favorable conditions In
prices to come within the present year,
and then the Bhlp-bulldlng Industry will
po forward with a spurt all over the
United States."

PROSPERITY IS
A STRONG CURD
FOR McKINLEY
Arthur Sewall Speaks
on Politics.
INFLUENCE OF CONDITIONS
Arthur M. Sewall. the multi-millionaire
thlp-bullder and famous silver advocate,
who has recently returned from the Ha
waiian Islands, when been yesterday and
questioned on the general condition of
business throughout the country, said:
"The general condition of business all
over the country is goodâ more prosper
ous, In fact, than it has been before in
years. Not only is this true of the condi
tion of trade in the United' States proper,
but the same good times pertain in our
tiewly acquired colonies and island pos
sessions. *
"I have Just returned from the Ha
waiian Islands, and things will boom
there as soon as Congress gives them a
regular form of government. The islands
ere Buffering to-day for want of
Congressional action. As soon as
the United States gives Hawaii a terri
torial form of government that will con
duct affairs under an American policy,
the new Territory will develop into one
of the most prosperous and best paying
properties we own.
"Honolulu, when Americanized and
cleaned up, will become the most popular
resort in this hemisphere. Aside from the
t&lsnds, the entire country is being swept
for the second best man, and he was
Thomas R. Bard.
Dunlap of Stockton declared the good
feeling of the supporters of Irving M.
Scott. Said he: â¢ . '
I voted during the first ssssion for a man
whose name is known throughout the land, a
man who Is famous for his wGrks and whom
I considered worthy to be California's repre
sentative In Congress. Under similar circum
stances I would vote for him again, .but the
conditions are changed and another man has
appeared as the choice of the people, and
bowing to their will it is my pleasure to sec
ond his nomination. Thomas R. Bard of Ven
tura is the choice of a majority of this Legis
lature and my faith In him is as great aB in
any candidate who has come before us. I
wish to add my voice to the chorus that Is
going up for him.
LARDNER SPEAKS FOR
THE OLD BARNES MEN
Assemblyman Lardher of Placer spoke
for the old Barnes supporters as follows:
Mr. Speaker and Members of the Assembly ;
You will all witness that I was a consistent
follower of that fearless and honorable W. H.
L. Barnes during the last session. On ac
count of infirmities I can simply say that the
friends of General W. H. L. Barnes resolved
to land him not only as a man to represent
one section of the State, but to represent the
whole State of California, but the time naa
come for every man who loves his State and
his party to lay aside personal preferences and
act for the good of his State and the great
partyâ the Republican party of the State of
California. Very often we resort to a second
choice and the Republican party away back
in Washington demands that this great State
should be fully recognized. Our. people began
to realize that, the time had- come that this
demand should be carried out. The Governor,
of the State eald it would be a crime if we
were not represented. It is needless to say
that all the trade of the Orient may come to
our ports, that we are asked to insure pur
ports, and that it becomes. a duty, a Bolemn
i duty, to assist In Â¦ bringing about this most
' happy result. We feel that the time has come
when we must lay aside our party feelings and
we must get together and select a United
States Senator. I have consulted a great many
of my constituents In Placer County. and they
are perfectly satisfied, and so I can say that
it gives me the most supreme pleasure to
second the nomination of- the Hon.- TMomas R.
Bard for United States Senator in the Con
gress of the Untied Statea. -- Â¦ Â¦

Mr. Speaker and Gentlemen of the Assembly:
These closing months of the greatest century
of all ages are tilled with marvelous changes
In the affairs of mankind. The genius of men
and the activities of nations have compelled
historians and geographers to glide their pens
swiftly in 'order to record the transformations
wrought by art, by science, by Industry and
by war.
Foremost among the nations thus moving for
ward tp nobler i achievements and a grander
destiny stands the United States. While un
dertaking by a wise Industrial policy to beÃtow
peace and prosperity on all within her borders,
ehe la also carrying the blessings of liberty
abroad. Yonder In the populous isles < of these
western seas we are struggling to pacify, to
educate, to civilize and to uplift.
These are j^tlrrlng -times. In the
western pathway .of commercial, moral
and national development under the Anglo-
Saxon banner stands California, fruttful in
products, rich In resources and boundless In
possibilities. If we will but rise equal to the
occasion and appropriate the opportunities
within our reach. . ,
Although the nation has been in ,the throes
of external war and internal industrial devel
opment, and although California Is In the path
way of this Oriental trade, yet for nearly a
year Bhe has sat as a half-orphan In the high
est council of the nation. '.
For thirteen months we' have experienced a
most bitter contest in seeking a United States
Senator who will fully represent California in
the sisterhood of States. Personal prejudices,
vicious articles, defamatory words and fac
tional party bickerings have rent the Republi
can party and Injured the State while the. un
seemly contest waged.

and In the face of opposition sought to Impugn
his pure motives. Governor Henry T. Gage

calm council to select some gentleman for the
United States Benate unquestionably worthy
of the high honor, whose ability and Integrity

the SDlendid and . progressive views of the
I'resldent and who when elected will . un
swervingly assist the national administration
In its magnificent governmental plan of ex
tending American civilization and commerce
to the new territory, where the American sol
dier and . sailor have planted and maintained
our country's flag." ' " ' ' .
To-day we meet In extraordinary session to
perform that duty for which we were elected.
To-day I have the honor to present to you for
your consideration the name of a gentleman
who meets all the conditions of Governor
Gage and our Ideal.
That gentleman is Thomas R. Bard. " ; .
Summoned from his roses, from his ; groves
and his oil wells in the southland, he comes
loyally Clnclnnatus-like, ready with his whole

BARD'S FAMILY NOT
GOING TO WASHINGTON
New Senator Requested to T>eliver
Address afc Los Angeles Chamber
of Commerce Banquet.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LOS ANGELES. Feb. Â«.â ln the selec
tion of Thomas R. Bard as the junior
United States Senator from California Los
Angeles is again honored In having for
two consecutive terms a representative in
the national Senate. Thomas R. Bard has
been a resident of this city for nearly two
years, his wife and family being now
domiciled in 957 West Adams street,
the fashionable residence section. This
information has not become generally
public. -. .-; ,
When Mrs. Bard was seen to-night she
stated that Mr. Bard had informed her
of the result of - to-day's balloting and
that many friends had tendered tele
graphic congratulations. "I shall expect
my husband about Saturday," Mrs. Bard,
in. response to on inquiry, said, "but I
know nothing of his intentions,' although
it was understood when he went to Sac
ramento that should he become the choice
of his party he would start for Washing
ton as soon as his business affairs could
be arranged. I do not expect to go to
Washington with Mr. Bard, and as far
as our plans are now known none of the
family will go. Whether Mr. Bard has
accepted any of the invitations that have
been extended to him I am unable to say.
but it will be fair to presume that he will
respond to the requests that it is reported
have been made by the citizens or Los
Angeles."
It is learned to-night that Mr. Bard
has been requested to, be the guest of
honor and to deliver an address at the an
nual banquet of the Chamber of Com
merce on Washington's birthday. An in
formal non-partisan reception will be-ten
dered Mr. . Bard . Saturday of thl3 week
should he return by that time. .

Bees are said to see an enormous, dis-
tance. When absent from their hive they
go up in the air till they see their homo

Carnegie and the Reporters.
One of Mr. Carnegie's weak spots Is his
fondness of newspaper men. says the New
York Press, particularly those in Pitts
burg. He used to know every one by
name, and never refused to see them and
listen to their inquiries.
I recall an Instance: There was a great
meeting in Glasgow about the time when
Mr. Carnegie was coaching through Scot
land rvith Mr. Biaine as hi? guest, and a
Pittsburg reporter happening to be In the
"center and crown of the shipbuilding
business of the world" decided to send to
his paper a cable dispatch concerning tho
reception of the distinguished American.
But the operator refused to receive It.
not knowing the sender, and the latter
appealed to Mr. Carnegie, who Immediate
ly, straightened things out. The cost of
the message was $4i>. and the newspaper
man. learning that his friend paid for it.
has sent a check for that amount to Mr.
Carnegie every year sinre. enly to have>
it returned with some humorous comment.
PELTED WITH BAD EGGS
FOR INSULTING A LADY
Severe but Deserved Punishment Meted
Out in Answer to a Note Written
by Frank L Gardner.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SANTA BARBARA. Feb. 6.â Frank L. Gardner, a representative of H.
Bohls & Co.. was rotten-egged by twenty of the most prominent young mea
in thi3 city to-night. He had written a very Insulting letter to one ef the
young ladies of this place, requesting her to meet hiia and go out for a
time. The letter was placed by the young lady in the hands of friends, who
sent him a decoy letter designating a meeting place. At the appointed time.
Gardner showed up and was given a severe pelting with rotten eggs. The
young men hope that it will serve as a lesson to a few others who are known
to be doing the same business.
grass. A party of Boers dispatched to
the scene was attacked from the trenches
and all retreated except four, who, hiding,
permitted the British to advance to within
fifty yards, when they fired a volley, kill
ing three men. This was seen from the
British fort, which fired a shell and sent
cavalry to assist the British. The Boers
beat these back, killing two men.

LONDON*. Feb. 7.â Lord Roberts'
customary phrasÂ», "No change in
the situation to-day," which alter
nate with the censor's "Situation
is unchanged," was posted earlfer
than usual at the War Office yesterday.
"When I entered the lobby at about 5
o'clock last evening the only occupants
were two brawny constables, who had re
moved the-lr helmets and were standing,
surveying with contented gaze the blank
bulletin board and empty room. At 11
o'clock the conditions were the same.
Absolutely no news of importance from
the seat of war was allowed to reach
What General Buller is doing is a sealed
mystery, but a hint is given in a telegram
from Ladysmith, of Sunday's date, via
Estcourt, which states that the Boers
were evidently in a state of alarm to the
east of the besieged city, evidently ex
pecting an attack.
In the north of Cape Colony, while there
Is no fresh intelligence, there is no doubt
that General Gatacre is about to begin
some hard fighting and make a move
ment simultaneously with Generals
French, Kelly-Kenny and Brabant.
As to hcrne affairs, the decisive vote on
the amendment to the reply to the Queen's
speech In favor of the Government in the
House of Commons last night and the
result of the York Parliamentary election,
where a Unionist was elected by an over
s-h^lmirsg majority to succeed Lord
Charles Beresford. are taken to Indicate
that the country Is standing behind Lord
Salisbury's Cabinet and will continue to
do fo until the war is brought to a suc
cessful conclusion.
Â»
KRUGER TO BLAME,
DECLARES ASQUITH
LONDON. Feb. 6.â ln the House of
Commons to-day, replying to a question
of John Dillon. Nationalist member for
East Mayo, as to whether the Government
intended to treat food stuffs as contra-
band during the present war and if so un
der what restrictions and limitations, Mr.
Balfour, First Lord of the Treasury, said
that food stuffs, as such, had never been
considered contraband by Great Britain.
But, he added, f%od stuffs intended to
fcupply the army In the field were, by the
universal consent of international law
yers, regarded as contraband, and a prize
court would decide the facts connected
with the shipments.
Asked If the Government Intended to
direct cruisers to seize all cargoes for
Lourenzo Marquez until their character
was determined by a prize court, Mr. Bal
four declined to make any statement in
this connection.
George W. Wyndham. Parliamentary
Secretary for the War Office, re
plying to a question, said there
was reason to .believe that many
of the guns from the forts at Pre
toria had been sent to the front, and that
some of them had a range of over 10,000
yards. The British siege train sent out
in December, he added, had guns of equal
range. *
The debate on the amendment to the
address in reply to the speech from the
throne was resumed by H. H. Asquith.
Liberal (Home Secretary in the Rosebery
Ministry), who from the tenor of his re
marks might have held a brief for the
Government. He maintained that the war
was neither intended nor desired by the
Government, which naturally explained
the country's unpreparedness. Asquith
added that the war could have been
avoided by President Kruger accepting
the proposals made in September, which,
according to the speaker, were perfectly
Compatible with the maintenance of the
independence of the Transvaal and a
proper installment of long delay in justice
to the British population. If he thought
the British negotiations were a mere
cloak to attack the Independence of or an
nex the Transvaal, then in spite of the in
vasion of British territory and the loss of
British lives he could not have reconciled
his conscience with voting a penny for the
prosecution of the war. But that is not
the opinion of the House of Commons
nor of the great majority of the country.
That was why they were united in Par
liament and a united people. Doubtless
there had been a demonstrable lack of
foresight and insight. Chamberlain, no
doubt, would give much for a chance of
re-editing his dispatches. But there was
no ground in anything which had hap
pened, or anything which he could con
template at present, for the "croakings"
of pessimists or the outbursts of panic.
Th* speech of Asquith almost caused a
sensation in the House. Asquith repre
sents the Roseberyites. and Lord Rose
bery was present in the gallery to hear
him. The debate for some time after the
conclusion of Mr. Asquith's remarks was
very flat.
Answering a question before the House
of Commons, A. J. Balfour. the Govern
ment leader, said there was no truth In
'.he story of Major General Sir William
Butler's communications to Sir Alfred
Milner. the British High Commissioner to
South Africa, as published by the Man
chester Guardian to-day. According to
the story referred to^ Major General But
ler, the former commander of the Brit
ish forces In South Africa, objected to re
inforcing the border garrisons, and said
that SO.uX) men should be on hand at the
outbreak of the war, even if it was con
fined to .-*'.,= South African republic.
John Burns, Radical member for Bat
tersea, congratulated the opposition on
having the courage to do its duty, "un
influenced by newspapers owned by black
guards and edited by ruffians who clamor
lor war." He characterized it as absurd
to say that the debate would be an en
couragement and an incentive to the
Boers.
"The Boers don't need an incentive,"
exclaimed Burns. '"This was supplied
them years ago by the blundering policy
of the Colonial Secretary, whose speech
might be fairly summarized? as follows:
'There is no argument for war. There
fore wave the Union Jack. A few mis
takes have been committed. Therefore
double your army In South Africa.' "
Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman, the
Liberal leader. Justified the opportune
ness of the amendment, because it was
the duty of the opposition to record its
judgment. He said the Government would
hardly pose as conquerors upon whose
brows laurel wreaths should be placed,
for their diplomacy had failed and their
military operations had been unsuccessful.
These facts. Sir Henry declared. Invited
criticism. At the same time he would
deprecate exaggeration and would deny
the existence of national humiliation and
discomfiture. He also denied that the
opposition was actuated by a desire to
clamber into power or to seek party ad
vantage. The "composed and sensible
tone of the speech of the Colonial Secre
tary," he haa been glad to hear. If the
Government were aware of the disparity
between the defensive forces of the South
African colonies and the military strength
of the Boers when the negotiations were
entered upon, no condemnation, in his
judgment, could be too severe. Sir Henry
ridiculed Lord Salisbury's statement that
the secret service fund was too small and
pointed out that the whole sum had not
been expended. He thought the Govern
ment should have disavowed the Jameson
raid and given guarantees for the future,
as Great Britain would have then been in
a position to demand that Boers cease
arming.
Sir Henry demanded to know upon what
evidence the existence of a Dutch con
spiracy was asserted, declaring that, un
less the charge was well founded, it was a
most wanton insult to her Majesty's loyal
Dutch subjects at the Cape. The war
must be prosecuted with vigor, he main
tained, but provided British territories
were free and Great Britain's military su-
Deriority was vindicated, what mattered

"What is the obpect of the amendment?
What is it likely to attain? One might

ment In our history."

position of wishing to oust the Govern
ment, a suggestion that was received
with considerable lauchter. He com
mented upon the diversity of views in the

"Is the opposition," he asked, "wise or
patriotic? What the country has to ' fear
is foreign nations,- and the Transvaal
politicians may think they can find their
opportunity in our dissensions. The
House of Commons cannot do much to
help our arms, but this it can do â It can
show that behind our soldiers in the field
there is a united country. (Loud cheers.)
"Can the members of the opposition re
flect with equanimity on the fact that
the vote they are about to give upon the
amendment may lengthen the war and
increase the tragic list of losses, and
probably add one fraction to the chances
of European complications?"
Mr. Balfour, in closing, appealed to the
members of the House, irrespective of

THE STARS AS TIMEKEEPERS.
They Are Much More Reliable Than
the Sun. :
Probably the majority of people sup
pose that the observatories obtain the
correct time from the sun. When the av
erage man wishes to give his watch the
highest praise he says "It regulates the
sun," not being aware that a watch which
would keep with the sun around the year
would have to be nearly as bad as Cap'n
Cuttle's. The farmer may safely decide
when to go in to dinner by the sun. but if
the mariner was as confident that the
sun marked always the correct time as
the farmer is he would be sure to be at
times two or three hundred miles from
where he thought he was. In other words,
the sun â that is, a sun dialâ is only cor
rect on a few days In each year, and dur
ing the intervening times gets. as far as
a whole quarter hour fast or slow.
These variations of the sun from uni
form time caused no end of trouble be
tween the astronomers and the fine clock
makers before it was discovered that the
sun time Is subject to such irregularities.
The better the clock the worse it often
seemed to go. . .-
But as the variations In sun time are
now accurately known correct time might
be obtained from the sun by making
proper allowance were it not for the dif
ficulty of observing its position with suf
ficient exactness. The large disk of the
sun cannot be located as perfectly as can
the single point which a star make 3. For
this reason astronomers depend almost
wholly upon the stars for obtaining accu
rate time. It is the method of doing this
winch we propose to describe.
There are several hundred stars whose
positions have been established with the
greatest accuracy by the most careful
observations at a number of the prin
cipal observatories ,of the world. If a
star's exact position is known it can read
ily be calculated when it will cross the
meridian of any. given placeâ that is, the
instant it will cross a north and south line
through the place.' The data regarding
these stars are all published in the nauti
cal almanacs, which are got out by sev-
Acquitted of Murder.
MODESTO, Feb. 6.â The trial of Andrew
Boss for killing Edward Purcell at Oak
dale -last October, was concluded in the
Superior Court this afternoon. The jury
brought in a verdict of acquittal. Bos 3
claimed self-defense and testified that
Purcell was coming toward him in a

when he shot. The jury wa3 out an hour.
Big Republican Rally.
ANGELS CAMP. Feb. 6.â Judson Brusie
of Sacramento and D. E. McKiniay of San
Francisco held a big Republican rally to
night. Both spoke at length on the issues
of the day and evoked frequent applause
from a large and enthusiastic audience.
After the meeting closed a Republican
club along national lines was organized,
with Frank Douglass as president and
Frank Egan as secretary.

TAKEN BY FILIPINOS
MANILA. Feb. s.â The . Insurgents at
tacked the First Battalion of the Forty
ninth Infantry near Xias. A major and
a captain were wounded and one ser
geant was killed, but details of the en
gagement are lacking.
Ships arriving from Legaspl report that
General Pana concentrated a large force
around the town and made a sharp night
attack on Major Shipton's battalion of
the Forty-seventh Infantry, which occu
pied a large convent as a fort. One bat
talion and a battery have sailed as rein
forcements for Shipton. The casualties
are unknown.
A corporal and four men while patrol
ing the railroad near Mabacalat recently
disappeared, and are supposed to have
been captured. A searching party look
ing for them was ambushed and a cor
poral killed.
AMERICANS AMBUSHED
AND SIX ARE KILLED
MANILA,; Feb. 7, noon.â The Insurgents
on Monday captured a supply train of nln e
bull carts between Oranl and Dinalupijan.
killing a corporal and five privates of
Company G, Thirty-second Infantry. The
escort consisted of a sergeant and eleven
mounted men. The insurgents weakened
the supports of a bridge over a creek and
the first cart went into the stream. While
the escort was bunched trying to haul
the cart out of the water, the insurgents
fired a volley from the bushes, killing the
six Americans and also two native driv
ers.

THREATENS THE BOER
RIGHT AT MAGERSFONTEIN
LONDON. Feb. 7. 4:20 a. m.â Lord Rob
erts has* caused to be distributed In the
towns bordering upon the invaded colo
nial territory an Invitation to Free Stat
ers .and Transvaalers to go Into the des
ert, offering them good treatment and a
restoration to their farms on the British
occupation of republican territory- The
manifesto guarantees that those bringing
horses can sell them. Foreigners will
have their passage paid to Europe. Co
lonial rebels are advised to surrender in
preference to being taken prisoners.
General Mac Donald, with 4000 infantry,
cavalry and artillery, threatens the Boer
right at Magersfontein. This is the first
sign of activity, on the part of Lord Me
thuen for some weeks and doubtless has
relation to the movements of the British
farther east. "
The Standard has received the follow
ing dispatch, dated Monday, February 5,
from Koppies Dam:
"General Mac Donald, with a brigade of
Infantry, a regiment of lancers and one
battery, has reached here from Modder
River camp, in what Is regarded as an
important movement to the right of the
Boer position at Magersfontein. Koppies
Dam commands the roads from Klmber
ley to Hopetown and Douglass. Two
Boer laagers, at Kamulhoek and Griqua
town, are within striking distance. The
arrival of General Mac Donald's column
was opportune, as it just prevented twb
large commandos effecting a luncture.
He now holds both banks of the river.
The lancers had two brushes with the
Boer patrols. There is great activity
among the enemy and strong forces are
concentrated to oppose our further ad
vance. The troops suffered from heat and
scarcity of water during the march."
WILKINSON'S MOST
SEVERE CRITICISM
LONDON, Feb. 7.â ln the absence of
war news this morning Spencer "Wilkin
son in the. Morning Post discusses the
problem of arming the nation at home.
He says:
"There is now such Intense and wide
spread interest in the army and its man
agement that the nation will have itself
to thank if the solving of the problem
should not be the history of the next few
months."
Ho proceeds to argue that "conscription
would be valueless unless the general
management of the army .were first re
modeled." On this point he says:
"A war office that for thirty years has
not known what to do with the volun
teers would not know what to do with the
half a million conscripts."
Mr. Wilkinson also discusses the use
of the bayonet, remarking:
"This weapon very seldom comes into
actual use. Its effect Is on the spirits of
both sides rather than on the bodies of
either. The British by using formations
fit for charging, but ndt for shooting.

BUCKEYE STATE REUNION.
Annual Banquet of the Ohio Society
of California.
About two hundred guests who are
proud to call the Buckeye State the land
of their birth gathered in the banquet
hall of the California Hotel last night to
Join in the annual Jinks of the Ohio So
ciety of California. The guest of the
evening was to have been Hon. F. X.
Schoonmaker, but in his unavoidable ab
sence the toast of "The Administration"
was given by General N. P. Chipman.
William H. Jordan acted as toastmaster
and opened the speechmaking with a trib
ute to the great State of Ohio and to some
of the notable people it had produced.
Rev. E. A. Woods proposed "Ohio, Our
Birthplace," and Major Grosbeck wel
comed the invited guests on behalf of the
society. Colonel J. P. Jackson gave the
toant "The Philippines," and voiced the
sentiment that the great man whom Ohio
had sent to the White House could be
safely trusted to handle the complicated
questions that our Eastern conquests haVe
brought up. "California, Our Home,"
gave ex-Mayor Davis of Oakland an op
portunity to say many good things of the
Golden State, and Rev. F. S. Brush
wound up the oratory with a few remarks
of the Presidents that Ohio had pro
duced.

Sold Liquor Illegally.
Special Dispatch to The Call
PASADENA, Feb. 6.â Having stamped
out the blind pigs, the polite have begun
to raid the drug stores which sell liquor
Illegally. It Is against the law to sell
liquor without a prescription, but the law
has been violated for a long time. Last
night Asbury G. Smith and J. W. Woods,
clerk, were arrested and arraigned, giv
ing bonds. Complaints were sworn to by
M. Richardson of Los Angeles, who came
from Los Angeles to have a good time.
The police had watched him and saw
where he got the stuff, then the Marshal
persuaded him to peach on his hosts.
New Hospital for Los Angeles.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PASADENA, Feb. 6.â The Roman Cath
olics plan to start a fine hospital In this
city. Mother Joakim, sister superior of
â¢Mercy Home, Los Angeles, and assistants
have been looking over the field and will
acquire for hospital use the old St. An
drew's Church, which waa abandoned
when the new brick church on North Fair
Oaks avenue was completed a few months
ago. They propose to use this temporarly
and later build a handsome edifice. The
hospital at San Bernardino will then be
transferred here.

FLY-CASTERS
NAME DATES
OF CONTESTS
Elect Officers for the Ensuing
Term and Fix Conditions
Governing Casting.
The annual meeting and election of offi
cers of the San Francisco Fly-casting
Club was held last night in the assembly
room, Mills building. AValter D. Mans
field, who has done so much toward the
development of the pastime of fly-casting,
was- re-elected to the presidency. The
other officers are: F. H. Reed, vice presi
dent; Horace Smyth, secretary; Herman
Muller, treasurer, Charles Huyck was
appointed acting secretary in the absence
of Mr. Smyth in the East. The executive
committee consists of Colonel George C.
Edwards, James S. Turner, John P. Bab
cock, Edward Everett and C. G. Young.
Tha . rules governing . contests and the
dates for this year's competitions, aa sug
gested by the executive committee, were
adopted with only minor changes. The
tendency throughout was to raise the
standard required to qualify for the vari
ous classes and to place the pastime on a
still higher plane. The contests will be
held at Stow Lake on the second and
fourth Saturday and Sunday in each
month until the Ist of October.
It was decided that contestants shall be
allowed five minutes to cast for distance,
with no time allowance for replacing a
lost fly. It was found that on an average
only half this time was required. To cor
rect an abuse which might develop it was
decided that not more than sixty feet of
line, including leader, shall be extended
beyond the tip of the rod when contest
ant makes his first cast. Contestants will
not be allowed to extend their lines along
the bank of the lake more than sixty feet
during the casting. =-.
A. E. Lovett was made captain of the
Saturday contests and T. W. Brotherton
captain of those held on Sunday.
Dr. W. E. Brooks was elected a member,
while other names were received and
placed on the waiting list. The member
ship reached the limit of 100 some time
since, and, -like public servants, few die
and. none resign.

meeting of the board a resolution was
passed calling for the election of three
Trustees, Marshal, Clerk, and Treasurer,
to be held some time next month.
Trustee Sylva, the most notorious friend
of the Harvey-Harrison clique, states em
phatically that the places vacated by the
trio will be filled with a water front ticket
of gamblers' supporters. Who these are
Sylva refuses to state. On the other hand,
the hill-tribe contingent are equally con
fident of driving out the sports and enter
tain no doubt but that victory will perch
upon their banners. As the poolroom pro
prietors last year made an avowed profit
of $00,000 there is no likelihood of their
letting such a prize go without a struggle.
The election promises to be one of the
most exciting ever held here, as both sides
are determined to fight the matter to the
bitter end.

PICTURES WILL TELL THE
STORY OF A GREAT FIGHT
JEFFRIES AWAITS THE ONSET OF SHARKEY.
From a copyright photograph by the American Mutoscope and Biograph Company
THE . pictures of the championship fight between Jeffries and Sharkey
In the arena of the Coney Island Sporting Club on November 3 last will
be shown for the first time in this city at the Alhambra Theater next
Sunday afternoon. The film secured at the ringside by tho American
Biograph Company contains 216,000 distinct pictures, it being over seven miles
long.
The fight, from the time the first man entered the ring until the end came,
lasted just .100 minutes. Every move made by the principals in that time
is depicted on the screen. According to the friends of Sharkey, the sailor
received the worst of the decision from Referee Siler. In support of this
claim they offer the pictures of the fight in evidence. The pictures will
be shown afternoon and evening for two weeks. To. persons who have
never seen a prize-fight these views should prove of especial Interest.
The whole scene. is spread out before them vividly, lacking only the sounds
of the blows and the cries of the spectators to complete the effect. Â»'-

Soldier in Critical Condition.
Floyd Miller, a soldier recently from the
Philippines, is lying: at the Receiving Hos
pital In a precarious condition. He was
found last niffht in an unconscious condi
tion on Van Ness avenue, between Bush
and Pine streets, and sent to â¢ the hos
pital. He showed symptoms of poisoning:
and received the usual treatment from
the physicians in charge, but had not re
gained consciousness at an early hour this
morning. -It is believed that "knock-out
drops" were administered to the unfortu
nate fellow by persons who had designs
on his money. Miller was paid off to-day
and received $21. but whe-n he arrived In
the hoepltnl he had only. 65 cents on his
person. The police are investigating the
case.

BOXER M'DONALD TO
MEET "SOLDIER" GREEN
Jack McDonald, the young boxer who
won honors in the amateur championship
tournament at the Olympic Club last year,
will make his debut as a professional to
night. Ho will meet "Soldier" Phil Green
in the arena of the Columbia Athletic
Club on Golden Gate avenue to-night.
Green is a veteran of the ring and on his
past performances has been Installed a
strong favorite. At Harry Corbett's last
night the odds' were 10 to 6. In the part
mutuel betting both men were In demand,
although In this also Green was the more
favored.
There will be two preliminary bouts, the
opposing boxers being Joe Bell and Kid
Johnson and Billy Springfield and Charles
Thurston.

ucated, "v â- Â¦

followed' by George Collins of San Fran

HAVANA, Feb. 6.â Samuel Gompers,

Attempted to End His Life.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MARYSVILLE, Feb. 6.â J. F. Fincher
of Wheatland, a laborer, -was found lying:
unconscious in the lane leading from
Wheatland to Bear River about 10 o'clock
this morning. Finchar had severed the
main artery of his left arm and was slow
ly bleeding to death. He was taken to
Wheatland. The physicians have some
hope of his recovery. He gave no reason
for his act other than that he was tired
of life. Finchor has resided near Wheat
land for a number of years.
COURT NOTES.
Louise G. Kennedy was yesterday given
judgment by a Jury in Judge Hunt's court
against John Lee for $10,000. Lee, It was
proven, was the holder of mining stock
valued at $100,000, in which the plaintiff
had an interest valued at $10,000. The
mine, on which the entire valuation of
the stock was- placed, was recently sold
to Hayward & Lane, and the plaintiff
then demanded her share of the proceeds.
Her claim was repudiated and she filed
suit with the result as stated. .
Whether or not jurors when deliberat
ing on verdicts are entitled to their meals
will be determined soon by reason of a
precedent established by Judge Cook yes
terday. Ernest Fuhrman, charged with
the crime of grand larceny, occupied the
attention of a jury yesterday, and when
the hour for the noon meal arrived the
court, ordered the Sheriff to take the
twelve good men and true to luncheon.
A plea was made by the Sheriff that as
the charter made no provision for jurors'
meals if he fed them he would be out
that much in pocket. The court was ob
durate, however, and ordered the jurors
fed, and said that in event the Sheriff
failed to obey the order of court he would
call upon the Coroner to arrest him for
contempt of court. This threat had the
desired result, and now the Sheriff must
sue the city to recover the amount ex
pended in purchasing a banquet for the
jurors of Cook's court.
FIXED THE DUES OF THE
COMMERCIAL MUSEUM
ADVTSOBY COMMITTEE PASSES
ON GOVERNING BULES.
Discussed the Question of Delin
quents and the Bights of Mem
bers Who Go Out of
Business.
The advisory committee of the Pacific
Commercial Museum Committee on pro
motion met at S o'clock yesterday after
noon at the Board of Trade rooms to i
finally adopt the rules of government for
the proposed institution.
To provide for the raising of funds to
carry out the project it was decided that
the promotion committee should appoint
a finance committee, consisting of nine
members and sixty additional ones to so
licit subscriptions. These solicitors will
operate all over the Pacific Coast.
At the opening of the meeting a letter
from William P. Wilson, one of the direc
tors of the Philadelphia Commercial Mu
seum, was read. It extended the courte
sies of that body to any committee the
San trancisco organization might send
there.
The most discussion was held on the
subject of Initiation fees and dues. Mr. I
de Vouag'f idt-a. that members outside
this city should pay only half what those
residing here paid was voted down, it be
ing the opinion of the majority of those
present that all members should pay
alike. The dues were finally fixed at *6 a
month for all members, and a rule was
adopted that when any one was three
months In arrears he could be expelled at
the discretion of the board of governors,
which board could then proceed to en
force the payment by the defaulting mem
ber of all money due up to the end of his
Jive-year contract. It was decided that
the initiation fee Fhould be $C 0 to any one
Joining before the formal opening of the
museum and $IW> after that. This, It was
thought, would cause interested parties
to join at once. Dues will not begin to be
payable until after the opening, which it
was decided should take, place one year
from now.
The clause providing that no special
privileges- nor information should be fur
nished any one was adopted, with the
amendment that those caring to pay addi
tional for such privileges and information
could have them.
Mr. Sachs urged that a rule be adopted
providing that no member nor person go
ing out of business should be compelled
to pay dues afterward. He said some
firms and business men would not join
unless this was agreed to. This rule was
adopted.
The clause giving city members special
privileges in the matter of voting was
amended so all members could have equal
rights in that line.
The committee decided that the first
president, vice president and board of
governors should be Hected by the pro
moters ard should hold office for one,
year after the formal opening, after which
twenty -one governors should be elected,
seven of them to go out of office each
year.

TOMMY BURNS
IS SEVERELY
DEALT WITH
The California Jockey Club
Stewards Restrict His
Riding to Schorr Stable.
The touch of San Francisco high Ufa In
dulged in by Jockey Tommy Burn*, the
crack light-weight of the Middle West,
was pleasant and invigorating, but aa
events have developed altogether too ex
pensive. At a meeting held last evening
by. the board of stewards of the California
Jockey Club the riding of Burns. was re
stricted to the firms of Burns & W.ater
house and John F. Schorr & Son to May
4, and for the balance of the year
Tommy can pilot the horses of the Schorr
stable only. This ruling means the loss
of many hundreds of dollars to the young?
man, as- his services were In g^eat de
mand on the other side of the Rockies,
where he Is a big public favorite.
Burns is under contract to tho Mem
phis firm of J. F. Schorr & Son. at a
large yearly salary. Under the terms of
a special agreement the Jockey was al
lowed to come to California and ride for
Burns & Waterhouse, that firm enjoying
first call, even though the Memphis turf
men sent a stable here to race. Burns
scored a pronounced hit on his first ap
pearance at Oakland, immediately becom
ing a turf idoL Then . there came a re
action. His work became slovenly, and
It was hinted about that Tommy was a
too frequent starter in midnight handi
caps. To his credit, however, it can be
said, he did not pick his mounts, appar
ently being willling to toss the leg up on
."any old kind of a horse" that looked to
have a chance.
Shortly before the close of 1599 Burns re
fused to ride Sly in a race at Oakland,
when ordered to do so by the Judges. In
stead ho donned his street clothes, took
an early boat across the bay, and the
next day left on a duck-hunting trip. For
this breach, of turf etiquette he was indef
initely suspended, and until last evening
his fate was held in the balance. A ride
Tommy furnished on Dr. Bernays, when
that horse ruled favorite in the "betting on
December 23 last, caused considerable ad
verse comment at the time, and at last
night's investigation the rider was close
ly questioned in regard to it.
Among those called by the stewards
was Charley Ellison, the close friend and
adviser of Burns," who stated that on the
day Dr. Bernays was defeated he had bet
$200 on the horse. Ellison felt much down
cast over the trouble Burns found him
self in, and says he tried hard to get
Tommy to mend his ways. He made none
of the boy's engagements and seemed
more grieved over the whole affair than
the disciplined rider himself.
At the same meeting the stewards made
the suspension against Albert Songer In
definite. Songer was set down for sev
eral lazy looking saddle efforts.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL,, WEDNESDAY. FEBRUARY 7, 1900.
THIS CITY WILL
KEEP THE TRADE.
OF BAKERSFIELD
Los Angeles Defeated
on Tariff.
VALLEY RATES NOT CHANGED
Â«
LABGE VOLUME OF BUSINESS AT
STAKE.
The objectionable railway 'tariff that
would have practically shut San Francis
co out of the trade of Bakersfield and t;it=
criminated against this city as far norrh
in the San Joaquin Valley us Modesto will
not go into effect. Dispatches bringing
the news were received yesterday morning
from Messrs. Stubbs. Sproule and Bissell,
representing the Southern Pacific and
Sasta Fe roads. A teleprara received by
A. A. Watkins of the Boara of Tra.'.e
from J. C. Stubbs was as follows: "Pro
posed tariff to San Joaquin Valley points
will Dot be adopted. Publication was a
mistake." A joint dispatch was also re
eclvfd from Messrs. Bif=KÂ»>ll and Sproule,
which read: "Proposed tariff to which you
object will not be put in force." Similar
dispatches were sent to the Chamber of
Commerce and the Manufacturers' eirl
Producers' Association, those organiza
tions having united with the Board of
Trade in making: protest against the
tariff.
- This Fettles the matter, and also bring*
to a close an Interesting chapter of rail
way history in the State. Before the Val
ley road was operated to Bakersfield LjoÂ»
Angeles had an advantage in the matter
of freights over San Francisco, and so
was able to do a considerable business in
the San JotKjuin Valley. The Valley road
<Hd this city a service in lowering the
rates to Bakorsfuld. Recently the Ban
Francisco merchants have had an advan
tage on one class of freight and Los An
geles an advantage on another. About a
yoar ego the lx>s Anpeles merchants in
effectively tried to persuade the Southern
Pacific Company to put into effect sub
stantially the fsme tariff that by reason
of the. protest made by San Francisco has
just been shelved.
â¢ The- interests, involved are larger than
mig-ht be guessed by a casual .reader. It
is estimated by a;merchant, who is w-11
informed, that the trade of Bakersfteld
alone Is worth from IUEOOjOOO to-$2,000.Â»K>
to San Francisco per annum. The trade
of other points which were affected by the
tariff Is equally large, and San Francisco
stood to lose net less than $4/Â»0,000 in its
annual volume- of trade. The Los Angeles
people, already sore over the routing ques
tion, will be disappointed at the result of
the attempt to take away the trade terri
tory of San Francisco.

Died in a Buggy.
Special Dispatch to The Call
PASO ROBL.ES, Feb. 6.â Sam Wiener, a
native of Pennsylvania, who was visiting
friends near Creston In this county, waa
out hunting yesterday afternoon In a
horse and carriage and not returning at
night search for the missing man was in
stituted by the Ernst brothers, who havo
large ranches near Creston. This morn
ing the body of Wiener was found about a
mile southeast from the Geneseo school
house, lying In the bed of the carriage.
Heart disease Is presumed to have been
the cause of death. Deceased has a fam
ily at Geneseo, 111.

Mrs. James gave the result of twenty
years' observation on this subject while
on the Woman's Home Mission Board of
the Presbyterian church. She spoke in,
the most vigorous terms of denunciation
against the Mormon church, declaring it
was colonizing and extending Its work In
Montana, Idaho, Colorado Â¦ and other
States. . *

Christian Temperance Union said she ob
served how the Mormons were propagat
ing their faith everywhere. Travelers en
countered its apostles In Norway. Sweden.
Denmark and Germany and in the most

Mrs. Hamlin gave the result of extended
observations on this subject. She said
the god of Mormonism was polygamy:
that B. H., Roberts had so written, and
that this doctrine was taught in the Mor
mon Sabbath schools.
A letter from Bishop Hurst of thÂ«
Methodist Episcopal church was read
supporting the movement. .
The hearing will be continued at the
next meeting of the committee, when W.

MEETS DEATH
WHILE HUNTING
A Napa Man Accident
ally Shot.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NAPA, Feb. 6.â James McDonald, who
was the superintendent of the Napa
Woolen Mills, while out hunting to-day
accldently shot and killed himself. The
accident occurred near Napa Junction,
whither McDonald had gone duck hunt-
Ing in company with Peter John and tho
young son of the latter. Mr. John and
his son were sitting in tho boat, which was
anchored in midstream. McDonald had
gone ashore to get a shot at some ducks
that had alighted not far distant. Just
how the accident occurred la not
known. John and His son heard a report
from the gun, followed almost Instantly
by a second, after which they heard Mc-
Donald cry for help. Going to his assist
ance- they found the unfortunate man with
a terrible wound in his right shoulder.
At once young John went to Napa to
secure medical assistance and to notify
Mrs. McDonald. In the meantime the
eldpr John removed McDonald to the
wharf of the Cutting Packing Company,
where everything possible was done, for
the wounded man. He lived but about
N ]VlcDonaVd was a native of Rhode Island
and 30 years of age. Prior to coming to
Napa he was employed In the Albany
â¢"miÂ« of Oregon. He was a member of
Company A. Fifth Regiment. N. G. C..
and very highly respected in this city. Ht
leaves a widow and a small child.

SEEKS TO FASTEN
GUILT ON CORNISH
Molineux Defense Calls
No Witnesses.
NEW TORK. Feb. 6.â Counsel for Ro
land Molineux, charged with having: mur
dered Mrs. Katherine J. Adams by poison
sent to Harry Cornish in December, 1593.
took a bold and unexpected step to-day,
announcing that the defense would sub
mit no evidence. Last night Barlow S.
Weeks, counsel for Molineux, stated that
the defense would consume less time than
the prosecution. It had been the general
expectation that many witnesses would
be called. Including handwriting Expert
Carvalho and a number of persons who
would attack the character of Harry
Cornish. QÂ£3B
Weeks began his summing up to-day
and had not finished when court ad
journed. He spoke with great earnest
ress and the jury listened to him intently.
He told of his long personal friendship
Â¦with Molineux and he attacked Harry
Cornish bitterly. The tenor of his argu
ment was that- the proofs pointed more
strongly to Cornish as the murderer than
to Molineux. MoUneux's father, mother
and wife were in court, as was Cornish,
who was visibly nervous. Molineux
seemed cool and collected.
"No testimony has been produced to
show that this defendant mailed this
package or was in the vicinity of the
Postofflce at 3:30 o'clock on December 23.
IS9S the day this package was mailed,
but." said Weeks, pausing an instant and
poisins his hand in the air while his eyes
swept over the jury, "Harry Cornish was,
and he was at the Postofflce at exactly
the time the package was mailed and on
that day. That silver bottle nolder was
bought by a man who said that he wanted
It "for a lady's dresser* and matched the
eilver on that dresser. There is not a
syllable of evidence or scintilla of proof
that that package ever came through the
mails, except Mr. Cornish's evidence.
Has he told the truth? This is important.
No one proves the receipt, no postman
proves the delivery, except Cornish."
Word by word Weeks went over the
Cornish testimony and strove to impress
upon the jury the suspicion that Cornish
might have "been guilty. He dilated on
the suspicious circumstances.
"Mrs. Adams took a dosÂ»e of medicine
and died. Mr. Cornish took a dose, or
cays he did, on an empty stomach and
wanders around all day with no evil ef
fect. He says he took a dose. He even
went to see Yocum. a man who knows
how to make cyanide of mercury. Now
what reasonable excuse did Cornish have
for visiting Yocum that day of all days?"
Week* then described MoUneux's actions
ever eince the newspapers accused him
as those of an innocent man. \veeks
asked for an adjournment until to-mor
row, his voice having failed. The jury
will be locked up to-night in the Astor
House. Weeks' address will consume all
of to-morrow. _.

GAVE NO BANQUET TO
COUNT DE CASTELLANE
French Embassador to Washington
Finds It Necessary to Correct a
Misapprehension.
WASHINGTON. Feb. 6.â An authorized
statement is made on the best authority
that all thai has been published in the
press concerning an alleged banquet of
fered by Embassador Cambon of France
to Count <le Castellane Is inaccurate. The
matter has attracted considerate atten
tion on both sides of the water, but the
discussion has proceeded on an entire mis
apprehension of the facts. It appears that
the French Embassador was present at an
informal luncheon at one of the fasnion
ebie restaurants of Nsw 'iork. at which
Count and Countess de Castellane were
also Invited. The affair was purely social
end has been given an Importance entirely
disproportionate to Its formal character.

REPUBLICAN LEAGUE
OF OREGON MEETS
PORTLAND, Or., Feb. 6.â The Republi
can League of Oregon to-day held its
biennial session In this city, about SOO
delegates being present. The league
elected officers and fourteen delegates to
th National League. J. M. Church, a
banker of La Grande, was chosen presi
dent The following resolutions wero
adopted:
So long as either of the great political parties
advocates the free coinage of silver, the finan
cial question is the paramount question before
the American people. The Republican clubs
of the State of Oregon, in convention assem
bled, reaffirm their loyalty to the gold stand
ard and their opposition to the free coinage of
sliver.
We believe the best and mest stable dollar
in the world should be assured by legislation
to the laborer as the fruit of his toil and to
the farmer as the price of his crop. We are
in sympathy with the efforts of the Republican
Senators and Representatives in Congress to
enact legislation for this purpose.
We hereby heartily Indorse the Philippine pol
icy of the present administration. These islands
have been won by American blood and pur
chased by American treasure: they should re
main a permanent, part of our national domain.
We cannot neglect our duty to give them a
stable government, nor can we afford to throw
away the great opportunity they give us for
development of our Oriental trade.
The Republican party was formed for the
purpore of fighting the battle* of free labor.
Throughout its whole history it has been the
champion of the people and of the American
laborer In particular. We are opposed to the
monopolization of commerce and Industry by
trusts and are in favor of all Judicious legis
lation-looking to - the curtailment of their
power.
A resolution in favor of electing United
States Senators by a direct vote of the
people was adopted.
LAWS NEEDED TO
CHECK POLYGAMY
Appeal of Christians to
Congress.
WASHINGTON. Feb. 6.-The rooms of
the House Committee on Judiciary were
crowded to overflowing to-day with the
representatives of many religious, mis
sionary and social reform bodies to urge
the need of a constitutional amendment
prohibiting polygamy. Among those heard
were Bishop Satterlee. Episcopal Bishop
of Washington; Rev. Dr. Hamlin. In be
half of the Presbyterian church; Dr. S. H.
Green of the Baptist church. Mrs. Janes
for the Women's Home Missionary Board
of the Presbyterian church. Miss Jennie
Ackerman, traveling representative of the
Women's Christian Temperance Union;
Judge W. M. Springer of Indian Ter
ritory, Dr. John Dixon of New York and
Dr. L. A. Foote of Brooklyn.
The addresses were condemnatory of
polygamy, and many of those engaged in
missionary work in the West asserted that
the Mormon church was systematically
propagating the doctrine of polygamy,
despite all denials. The women speakers
appealed in particular for protection to
the sanctity of the American home.
Bishop Satterlee voiced the Episcopalian
sentiment by saying that the family was
the root of modern civilization, and be
earnestly supported this movement as one
In defense of the family and the home.
Rev. John Dixon of New York said the-
Mormon church was seeking to make a
virtue out of a vice and to establish this
vice as the cornerstone of religion.
Rev. T. S. Hamlin of the Church of the
Covenant, Washington, said that what
was feared was that with the disposition
of Roberts' case Congress would drop the
subject, whereas the religious sentiment
of the country wanted the movement to
bring forth a constitutional amendment
