TO ABATE A NUISANCE.
Citizens of Woodland Will Assemble
in Mass Meeting.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WOODLAND, Feb. 6.â The City Trustees
have called a mass meeting of the tax
payers of this city for Thursday evening
to consider a very important question.
The city sewer farm 13 located about two
miles northeast of Woodland. Last Feb
ruary T. L. Baird et al., farmers who live
In the vicinity of the farm, commenced
an action to have the farm declared a
nuisance and to collect $13,000 damages.
The case was tried before Judge E. C.
Hart of Sacramento nnd a jury. A ver
dict for $1000 damages and costs was ren
dered, but Judge Hart modified the ver
dict and rendered judgment for plaintiffs
in the sum of $70 and costs and restrairied
the city frÂ«n maintaining a sewer farm
at the present location. From this judg
ment the city took an appeal.
Plaintiffs now say that they do not de
sire/to collect damages and only want
the nuisance abated. A great many tax
payers are anxious to avoid any further
expense by compromising the case. It is
to consider this proposition that the meet-
Ing has been called.
BURGLARS IN NAPA.
Series of Crimes Alarm the Residents
of That City.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
j NAPA. Feb. . Câ Burglars are operating
in this city, and several attempts have
been made by thieves to enter dwellings
during the last few days. Sunday morn
ing last, between the hours of 11 and 12
o'clock, William Laws' residence on Cross
street was entered by burglars and $75 in
gold taken. The family was at church.
The local detective force, headed by Chief
of Police Frank B. Gibb, is making a
thorough search for. the robbers. The
theory is entertained that this work was
done by the same parties! who tried to
set fire to the Central School building.

LOS ANGELES, Feb. 6.â A new , patent

other men were painfully blistered.
EXPANSION AND
ITS DRAWBACKS
Argument of Williams
in the House.
Â»
WASHINGTON. Feb. 6.â The Philippine
question again occupied the attention of
the House to-day, with a slight digression
concerning the war in South Africa. The
feature of the debate was the speech of
Williams of Mississippi, who presented an
argument against the annexation of the
Philippines which attracted much atten
tion. It was devoted almost entirely to
the commercial aspects of the acquisition,
holding that the absorption of the Islands
would be ultimately ruinous to the Ameri
can producers of cotton, rice, tobacco,
hemp and sugar. Morris of Minnesota
made an exhaustive legal argument in
support of the right to hold and govern
the islands. The other speakers wer3
Gibson of Tennessee, V%". A. and H. C.
Smith of Michigan, Cochran of Missouri
and Neville of Nebraska. The general de
bate on the diplomatic bill closed to-day
and to-morrow it will be taken up for
amendment under the five-minute rule.
Until the financial measure now ?pending
before the Senate shall have been dis
posed of finally it will be considered by
the Senate every legislative day to the
exclusion of all except purely routine
business. This agreement will have the
effect of cutting off all debate on the
Philippine question or other matters ex
cept by unanimous consent of the Senate
unless Senators choose to discuss other
questions in the time they devote to the
financial bill. Caffery of Louisiana con
cluded his speech on the Philippine ques
tion to-day, holding that the United
States ought to restore in the islands the
status quo and then confer Independence
upon the Filipinos.

DAWSON BEING DESERTED
IN THE RUSH TO NOME
Adverse Legislation in the Matter of Re
locating Claims Paralyzing Business
in the Klondike.
By SAM W. WALL.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
DAWSOX, Jan. 9 (via Seattle. Feb. 6).â
Dawson Is suffering such an attack of
Nome fever that already the life has
cone out of the town. There are plenty
of living people here, to be sure, but they
are living upon the hope of being able to
ccc Nome. The only thought or the only
topic of conversation Is "The spring,''
"The breaking of the Ice in the river,"
that will enable them to move with
all they have to the new American
camp. Men are working on the creeks
at $5 a day In cash rather than ac
cept a higher wage "on bedrock"
cr "after the wash up." They want
money in hand so that they may be
free to leave the moment it Is possible to
do so by water. Merchants are closing
out their business already, and where this
time last year business places were in
demand ar.d rents goir.g up the sign. "To
let," meets one at every turn. Numbers
of men have left to make the long jour
ney of over two thousand miles over the
lee by dog team, and departures take
place nearly every day. The number will
Increase as the season progresses up to
the middle of February. The first of June
will find little of Dawson left but the
shell. Business is already dead here.
The past week has seen two of the
principal saloons close their doors perma
nently. Others will follow. The largest
of tho theaters and dance halls, the Pal
ace Grand, has closed, and the opera
house and Monte Carlo change hands
about every two weeks in a desperate ef
fort to fight o2 the inevitable. Charley
Meadows of San Francisco, known as
Arizona Charley, is proprietor of the Pal
ace Grand Theater. It is an immense
structure, with a large auditorium and
two tiers of boxes, ranging one above the
other, entirely round the house. He Is go-
Ing to undertake to .move the building
bodily as it stands to Nome. He has ac
tually let a contract for tho Job, and the
contractors have given bonds for its safe
delivery.
The building Is to be placed on four
large barges, to be built especially for the
purpose, and floated down the Yukon
to its mouth and thence, awaiting good
weather, towed by steamer to Nome.
The flight from Dawson In the spring
will far eclipse any stampede that ever
came this way. Literally everybody is
going who is .not too badly tied up here.
So uniform Is the rule that the man who
declares that he Is not going Is looked
upon as an oddity.
This condition of affair* is chargeable
entirely to pbnoxious legislation. The
Klondike is no longer a poor man's coun
try. The results of the years of pros
pecting and the location of pay streaks
have been taken away so far as the average
man Is concerned by a systematic course
of lawmaking directed in the interests of
the few. The recently adopted regulation
that prevents relocation of claims has
broken the camel's back. All claims now
located and that are allowed to lapse
through any neglect on the part of the
holder or other reason, and that formerly
in such event were open for relocation by
other miners, now, instead, revert to the
Government to be sold to the highest
bidder.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, FEBRUARY 7, 1900.
RATE FIXED FOR
FRUIT PACKING
Growers' Associations
Are Agreed.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN JOSE, Feb. 6.â The Associated
Fruit Packers and the California Cured
Fruit Association have agreed upon terms
for handling the dried prune .crop of the
coming year, and a contract to this effect
will be signed to-morrow. This action as
sures the success of the Cured Fruit As
sociation and means the price of prune*
will become stable and growers will re
ceive a fair remuneration . for their
products.
The Associated .Packers agree to take
the entire prune crop of the State and
pack and handle the same at the prices
to be fixed by the growers' association.
The Santa Clara County Fruit Exchange,
which heretofore has handled the bulk of
the crop, and the six or seven co-operative
driers throughout the county are put on
the same footing as the commercial pack
ers and will receive their share of the
prune crop to sell. The placing of the co
operative dryers and the commercial pack
ra was the only question on which It\was
feared a split might result, but this was
adjusted amicably.
The packers are to be allowed $5 per
ton for receiving, handling, storing, grad
ing and packing the prunes in flrst-clnss
bags'. In addition to this will be added
the sale price paid by the consumer.
The following prices for the different
packs have been decided upon: Twenty
flve-pound boxes, $10 per ton; fifty-pound
boxes, $5 per ton; eighty-pound boxes, $5
per ton. Different packs costing moro or
less than these amounts will be adjusted
by the two associations.
The Cured Fruit Association will havo
Inspectors at the various packing-houses
to guard the interests of the producer and
association to see that goods are
packed according to the rules. The con
tract also specifies that all fruit shipped
must bear the association's brand, which
is to the effect that the goods are as rep
resented. Five per cent is allowed the
packers for- selling, and they are not
allowed to give more thaji one-half of this
to the Eastern broker.
This contract between the packers ana
the association is not to go Into effect un
less contracts covering 90 per cent of the
prune crop is secured from the growers,
unless by mutual consent a less percent
age would be acceptable. The conditions
agreed to refer to prunes only. In regard
to other varieties of dried deciduous fruits
and nuts the association will not attempt
to maintain the price unless 75 per cent
of the crop is controlled, but will sell
these products for Its members to thn
best possible advantage.
The growers are rapidly signing con
tracts with the association, and nearly
the entire prune crop will be signed. The
drying plants of the various co-operative
fruit associations will be used to cure the
fruit, and growers will have no further
to haul their fruit than in other years.

NOVEL CLOTH COSTUME.
The dress represented Is of cloth In two
shades. It is in the princess style, but
gives the Impression of a jacket and skirt,
thanks to a stole of bright embroidered
drab cloth. The hips. are encircled with
a band of similar cloth, which descends
on each side of the apron. The skirt is
in fiat pleats, edged with a similar band.
The dress would look charming in either
amaranth or suede color. .

NATURALIZATIONâ S. P. A.. Santa
Rosa, Cal. A3 you came to the United
States from a foreign country, the land of
your birth, when you were a minor, re
sided in the United States for three years
next preceding your majority, have con
tinued to reside in the United States ever
since, and having also resided more than

your minority In this country, you may at
any time make application for and obtain
full naturalization papers, but at tha time
of declaration you will have to make oaih
that for two* years next preceding your
application it was your bon'a fide inten
tion to become a citizen of the United
States.

THE CALL AND THE SENATORSHIP.
THE successful -termination of the long struggle
to secure a United States Senator for Califor
nia is "a. subject for congratulation and good
feeling within and beyond party lines. The suffrage
of the people put upon the Republican party the se
lection,of a Senator. That duty carried with it a re
sponsibility to the whole State and an obligation to
that public opinion which sits in judgment upon par
ties and policies.
The Call bore its part in the fight' actuated by one
motive only. This paper at no time had any candi
date for the Senatorship. It sought only to secure
such a selection as would promote the self-respect of
every citizen and safeguard the interests of the State.
Our critics and our enemies, as well as our friends,
have never misunderstood our purposes.
We are anxious for the further success and con
tinued primacy of the Republican party, and are sen
sitive to the fame of California. Therefore we con
tested, step by step, all propositions, plans and cabals
formed for the purpose of unworthily bestowing the
Senatorship. This was done under the constant dan
ger of being accused of some form of self-seeking,
and sometimes this duty was discharged in the face of
other perils and in defiance of threats which the
strong use to appall the weak.
f We look back over the record with exactly the
! E3me sort of satisfaction that glows in enthusiastic
I expression throughout the State.
We hope that The Call is now better understood
than when the great fight began. We have opposed
r.o man whose election would have honored the party
and left unimpaired the magnificent strength it had
in 1898. Between t*e men worthy of the honor we
had at no time any choice. The selection of any one
of them, at any stage of the struggle, would have
commanded our support and indorsement. We have
conceived the function of a newspaper to be the de
feat, by all fair means, of any candidate who did* not
deserve the office and the honor, who had no equip
ment, mental or moral, for the great duties of Sena
tor, and whose election would hurt the State's pride
and bring the Republican party to present shame and
final defeat. That function The Call has discharged,
with conscience and courage. We have made no
effort to influence selection between gentlemen of
probity and fitness, but have stood between them as
a class and candidates who lacked either fitness or
probity, or both.
The record is made, and the Republican party and
the people of California now understand that The
Call fights no Republican who comes with clean
hands and purposes, and approved fitness, for the
public trust which he seeks. This paper is .not" a
broker in nominations, nor is it attorney in fact
for candidates. It is simply the implacable foe of that
immorality and ur.fitness which seeks sometimes to
artfully impose itself upon a successful party. As far
as the Southern Pacific Railroad is concerned, its
part in the contest was a mistake from which neither
the party nor the State will suffer, for both have
proved immune to its influence. The corporation
must bear alone the consequences of an act it had no
right reason for committing.. As a transportation
company the people are willing to be at peace with it.
Such business issues as arise between it and
its patrons the latter are willing to adjust in
a business way. When demagogues attack it,
and by threats arid appeals to prejudice en
deavor to force it into politics for defensive
purposes, the people are willing to keep it out of
politics by such a defeat of demagogy as they ad
ministered in 1898. Why not let it rest at that? Why
propagate a new spite in every unsatisfied political
ambition? Why seem to compel the best elements in
the State to mount guard continually to protect
themselves against some personal vagary of some
body connected with the railroad who, for reasons
that have no right relation to its real interests, tries
to impose unwortn and unfitness upon a place of
honor to transform it -into a place of shame? When
the Southern Pacific Company, within the limit of its
proper interests, needs defense we shall not scruple
to defend it. and when outside that limit it assumes to
do or to dictate where its presence is an affront, we
shall not scruple to fight it in the future as we have
given it battle in the past and have administered to
it the chastening admonition of defeat.
Having accomplished the task set for ourselves, in
the defeat of every candidate who did not deserve to
succeed, and whose success would have killed the
party and degraded the" State, The Call rests in the
company of the wise and upright for whom it has
spoken and wrought, ready to again clear the field of
the enemies of good government and leave it to the
victorious friends of that righteousness which" exalts
a party and gives it life in the confidence of the
people.

A LESSON FROM SOUTH CAROLINA.
pZjOR many years Georgia claimed to be the em-
I pire State of the South. Her people, proud of
A the success achieved in the upbuilding of At
lanta and of several other cities of hardly less note,
fondly believed their State would continue to hold
the title undisputed. They have been startled to find
themselves outstripped in the matter of cotton manu
facturing "by ,South Carolina, and are busily engaged
in considering how it happened.
There are other folks than Georgians interested in
the subject. The development of the manufacturing
industry of South Carolina has been,. in fact, one of
the marvels of the last decade. The people are poor
they have no large cities, they have but little banking
capital, they have had no experience in factory work,
their advantages in the way of coal are less than those
of many other States, and they have nothing of su
periority in water power. Nevertheless they have
made such progress in the manufacture of cotton that
when certain large* additions now in process of con
struction are made to the existing mills South Caro
lina will rank second only to Massachusetts as a cot
ton manufacturing State.
A record of that kind is an interesting fact in the
history of American industry, and is well worth a
study. The Atlanta Constitution, for the' purpose of
disepvering the cause of South Carolina's progress,
sent a special correspondent to make a tour of the
State and carefully investigate the means by which
the work has been accomplished. He has made his
report to that paper, and it is as well worth reading
here as in Georgia.
South Carolina exempts cotton factories from tax
ation for a period of years, but the Constitution cor
respondent found that the exemption has. had little to
d 6 with the increase of her factories." The exemption
law was in force for many years, but no one took ad
vantage of it. Outside capitalists were indifferent to
it, and it can hardly be said to have brought a single
dollar to the manufacturing interests of the State.
A few years ago the people of South Carolina

OUR HOME STUDY CIRCLE.
A GRATIFx ING evidence of popular satisfac
tion in the Home Study Circle of The Call-is
shown by the degree of interest taken in the
fpring course, which will begin on- Thursday, Feb
luary 15. The new series of studies will, we believe,
be found to be even more interesting than that which
has been running during the fall and winter. At any
rate the series is to include studies of subjects which
arc pertinent to the issues of the time, and will there
fore have a seasonable value in addition to their in-
The chief events of the year outside of the war in
South Africa are to be the Presidential election in the
United States and the universal exposition in Paris.
Relating to tbe first, the Home Study Circle will
have a series of papers on American political par
ties; while, for the benefit of those who design a
trip to Paris, or who for any other reason desire a
fair knowledge of the subject, there will be a course
of articles giving twenty lessons in French conversa
tion.
The other topics to be treated will be hardly less
interesting to the general public. Literary culture is
to well nigh universal in the United States that in al
most every home there will be one or more members
of the family who will find delight in the comprehen
sive course to be given in the papers on the Golden
Ages of Literature. The articles on Recent Scien
tific Discoveries will reveal a large amount of new
information on scientific subjects even to those who
have kept themselves fairly well posted on the extra
ordinary advance made by scientists in recent years.
Then the other topics, Photography for Amateurs
and Biographical Studies for Girls, will appeal to
thousands of readers outside of those for whom they
tre specially designed.
It wi'l be perceived that the spring course includes
Studies in American politics, the history of literature,
French conversation, scientific discoveries, pho
tography and a notable series of biographical articles
upon the lives and work of eminent women. There
ere six courses, one for each day of the week, ex
cluding Sunday, so that the same daily opportunities
for home study given during the fall and winter will
be carried through the spring term.
The contributors to the series are among the most
eminent educators and writers, of the country. The
course on American politics will be under the direc
tion of Jesse Macy, LL. D., professor of political
science of lowa College; Professor Benno Kirsch
baum, a distinguished French scholar and teacher,
trill prepare the instruction in French conversation:
the articles on scientific discoveries will be under the
direction of Professor William J. Hopkins of Drexel
Institute; George W. Gilson, editor of the Profes
sional Photographer, will have charge of the studies
in amateur photography; the biographical studies
â¢uill be under the supervision of Mrs. Charlotte
Erewster Jordan, author of "Royal Women of Euro
pean History"; and Edward Everett Hale of Boston,
Dr. Parrott of Princeton, Professor Palmer of Yale,
Professor More of Harvard, Professor Smith of the
University of Wisconsin, Miss Vida D. Scudder of
Wellesley, will contribute the papers on literature.
To each series of papers there will be many able
and brilliant contributors in addition to those named.
The whole spring course, in fact, will be one of
marked interest, and will add much to the value of
TWe Call as a family newspaper.

TRADE AND THE METRIC SYSTEM.
OWING to the intense competition of commerce
in these days and the closely matched powers
of the - principal competitors, any little advan
tage which one may have over another becomes a
matter of no slight importance. Consequently each
nation studies carefully the commercial system of the
other and takes note of every factor that affects the
world's market. Out of that condition of affairs has
come a revival of an agitation for the adoption of the
metric system of weights and measures in Great
Britain, for it appears that on the continent of Europe
the preference of the people for that system of
measurement constitutes a heavy handicap on the
sale of British goods in many of the Continental mar
kets.
The British Consul at Amsterdam is reported to
have recently directed the attention, of his country
men to some of the results of the handicap. It ap
pears the German manufacturers of iron and steel
have adopted a uniform scale of dimensions for cer
tain articles greatly in, demand, like steam boilers
and angle iron. These products arc carefully grad
uated in sizes for the trade, and the measurements all
conform to the metric standard. The latter already
prevails pretty generally on the continent of Europs.
Buyers there find it more convenient, consequently,
to patronize the German manufacturer than his
English rival, who adheres to the old system. For
bridge and other railway material, according to this
report, the preference is given to Germany; and in
the matter of pipes and tubes for water works "it is
absolutely certain that the Dutch market is com
pletely lost to Great Britain from the s?,me cause."
Incidentally it is added that a combination of Belgian
iron manufacturers has started works in China,
"where probably the Belgian system, and therefore
the metric, system, will be introduced."
Now that we have become large exporters of man
ufactured goods that information is of almost as
much interest to us as to the British. It seems in-,
evitable that the business world must come into some
sort of harmony in such matters. As all great com
mercial countries have found it of advantage to adopt
a common gold standard for their monetary systems,
so they will have to use a common system of weights
and measures. The many advantages the metric sys
tem has over that which prevails in the United States
and Great Britain renders it almost certain it will be
the system which will be accepted. Neither the
United States nor Great Britain, nor both combined,
can force their clumsy medieval method of weighing
and measuring upon the world in the face of the ex
cellent decimal system which the French have intro
duced and made popular throughout Europe. Sooner
or later we must come to it, and it would appear that
the sooner we do it the better.
THE gasoline schooner Nettle Low was capsized off
Bolinas In yesterday's northwester. Luckily the
captain, crew and passengers were saved, but the
vessel will probably be a total loss.
The Nettle Low left here at 8:45 o'clock yesterday
morning In charge of Captain T. G. Low. His crew con
sisted of his brother, J. A. Low, as engineer and Charles
Magruder. Mr. Bishop, a civil engineer connected with
the lighthouse department, and a Portuguese were passen
gers. There was a fair wind and no sea and no thought
was given of danger. When off Bollnas the wind fresh
ened considerably and was blowing nearly fifty miles an
hour at Point Reyes. A sudden puff of wind from the
shore caught the sail and keeled the vessel over and an
unusually large swell threw the schooner on her beam
ends. The passengers and crew took to the lifeboat and
rowed ashore, three miles distant. They reached land
safely and J. A- Low mounted a horse and rode to BoUr.aa
for assistance. Captain Low and.Magruder then roved
back to the vessel and anchored her and then waited for
a towboat. The anchor dragged, however, and the
schooner drifted to the shore. The brave skipper and Ms
assistant were carried out to sea, but were picked up by a
steam schooner bound north.
"When the towboat arrived Its services were not re
quired. She Is now lying on the north side of Bollnas
Point a total wreck. The Nettle Low was valued at J6OOO.
She carried butter from Point Reyes to San Francisco and
was well known on the bay.
The schooner Abbie also suffered during the north
wester. Her foresail was blown away and other damage
done. Shs cut back to port during the day.

of a commonwealth.

THE SAJS T Fll AX CISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, FEBRUARY 7, 1900.
adopted another method. They began to build small
factories with local capital, and as soon as these were
proven to be profitable Northern capitalists hastened
to take stock in them and to share in the profits. It
is said that "last year, when no benefits or induce
ments were offered or applied for, more capital was
invested than in the thirteen years when State, county
and city taxes were either refunded or remained un
collccted."
The Constitution says: "Local confidence, local
energy and local 'enterprise' attract the attention of
outside capitalists who have money to invest, and
this is the secret of the- tremendous advance which
South Carolina has made in the direction of cotton
manufacturing. Our correspondent says that there
is not a courthouse town in that State where from
$50,000 to $100,000 cannot be raised for investment in
a cotton mill. , This is all the incentive that outside
capital needs. When capitalists see comparatively
poor people ready to club their small savings together
and put it in any particular industry their conclusion
is that the risk must be exceedingly small, and so
they follow the example set."
There is a lesson in that for Californians as well as
for Georgians. We have no cotton, but we have
much raw material that now goes East to be manu
factured. It will never be manufactured here until
we begin the work. When we prove the industry to
be profitable, Eastern capital will. flow in. California
is far richer than South Carolina, but in how many
"courthouse towns" can we raise $100,000 to start a
factory for the manufacture of the raw products of the
locality?

PLANS FOR THE
ACQUISITION OF
ALL UTILITIES
Supervisors Urged to
Take Action.
REPORT OF CHIEF ENGINEER
? .
BOARD OF WORKS ASKS FOR AN
APPROPRIATION.
The Board of Supervisors yesterday re
ceived a communication from. City Engi
neer Grunsky in regard to a resolution
requesting the Board of Works to formu
late a plan under which the city may
gradually acquire its public utilities. The
report In part reads as follows:-
It is required by the charter that plans and
estimates be made of the cost of obtaining from
all of the available sources a sufficient and per
manent supply of good pure water. Taking tha
Investigations made by this municipality In
1876 as a guide It may be safely assumed that
ten independent sources of supply will have
to be examined. It will be necessary to investi
gate the quantity of water available for the
city's use. Its quality, storage facilities, con
duits for its delivery and Its distribution. The
minimum supply must be Investigated and it
is desirable that such Investigation should ex
tend over a number of seasons. The projects
will all Involve the crossing of tho bay with
a pipe line. It will be Impossible to reach final
conclusions within the year prescribed by the
charter, as little or no money can be raaiio
available to start actual work befora tho com
mencement of the next fiscal year.
It may be advisable to proceed by a process
of elimination and to determine on a basis
of a preliminary Investigation upon which
sources of supply final reports are most desired.
The projects to be examined and the order of
their Importance should be designated by the
Board of Supervisors, possibly after conference
with the Board of Works.
To further this end It would be advisable to
Issue a call for proposals to furnish water to
the city to request evidence as to the suffi
ciency of the supply and quality and the feasi
bility of Its delivery from the person or cor
poration offering the same. Similar steps
might also be taken In the matter of street
railroads so that the routes to be examined may
be designated. If this plan Is followed It Is
probable that a preliminary report can be made
within the time limit, provided that ample
means be placed at the disposal of the Engi
neer's office.
The following: estimate of the cost of
making the necessary surveys, plans and
reports is submitted, subject to correc
tion:
Water supply, on the assumption of six
projects to be examined 170.000
Gas works 7,600
Electric Hcht works, steam, water or
electric power works, telephone lines.. IS.OOO

VALLEY ROAD ABOUT THROUGH.
Construction of the Line Rapidly-
Drawing Toward Completion.
Work on the Valley road Is going right
ahead, and If the good weather continues
the middle of April will see the first load
of freight and passengers landed in San
Francisco.
The road has now been laid four miles
this side of Cornwall, which leaves the
comparatively small gap of three miles
to be covered before the rails will extend
In one unbroken line from Stockton to the
great Franklin tunnel. The tunnel once
reached, the ties can be placed and the
rails laid through it as soon as the con
tractors desire, as the tunnel is In entire
readiness for their reception. It . will
probably be a week or ten days before
the rails are put In and trains running
through the big hole In the hill. The
twenty-one or twenty-two miles between
Franklin tunnel and Point Richmond are
all graded and only waiting for the plac
ing oT the track. This work will be
pushed ahead as quickly as construction
can be accompfished.
The heavy work of the Point Richmond
wharf is all finished and the men are now
employed on the sllp3 and aprons. This
will take them about a month or six
weeks, when everything will be in readi
ness for the reception of the Ocean Wave,
which will do the water transportation
until the completion of the new boat now
building at the Union Iron Works.
RULOFSON IS NOT
HALL'S ADOPTED SON
JXJTKJB BELCHER FINDS HE TTAfI
NO LEGAL CLAIM.
Executor of Decedent's Will Is Given
Judgment for the Property and
His Co9ts of Action.
Alfred C. Rulofson Is not the legally
adopted son of the late Captain Winslow
G. Hall. Neither is he entitled to tho ed
tate of the deceased. Such are the con
clusions of Judge Belcher, expressed In a
decision Hied yesterday. Mr. Rulofson
who Is a son of William H. Rulofson a
member of the late firm of Rulofson &
Bradley, filed suit shortly 'after the death
of Captain Hall to recover decedent's es
tate on the theory that he was the legally
adopted son of the deceased, and although
not mentioned In his will was in fact en
titled to the 'entire estate. George 10.
Billings, as executor of the decedent's
will, made answer to the demands of thÂ«
plaintiff and denied that the claimant had
ever been adopted by the deceased, ani
he prayed in his answer that the claims
of the plaintiff be denied and that defend
ant be given Judgment for his co3ts.
In concluding his opinion Judge B-lcher
said that upon the facts and circum
stances adduced In evidence the plaintiff
has not established the allegations of his
complaint, and the finding must be that
the plaintiff is not the adopted son of
Captain Winslow G. Hall; that Captain
Hall did not agree to make hi/n his heir,
or at his death, or at any time, by vill
or otherwise, to leave him his entire prop
erty, or any of It. or anything.
In conclusion the court orders that tho
plaintiff take nothing by his action an-1
that the defendant take his proper costs.

EDWARD AIGELTINGER
MAY SUCCEED MAHONY
Friends of the Ex-Supervisor Want
Him Appointed a Member of the
Board of Public Works.
Owing to the death of his mother.
Mayor Phelan has not announced the suc
cessor to Mr. Mahony of the Board of
Public Works. A great deal, of specula
tion is rife among the politicians and
business men as to the man who will be
appointed to the position. As a vacancy
must be filled by a Republican, a great
deal of conjecture Is being had over the
probable appointee.
Edward H. Aigeltlnger. a member of
the last Board of Supervisors under the
old charter, has been prevailed upon by
leading citizens to offer his name for the
place. Prominent merchants of this city
are outspoken in their indorsement of
Mr. Aigeltlnger for the place, because of
his being a practical business man and
one who was always ready to discuss
matters that came up before the old
board In a strictly businesslike manner
As chairman of the Street and Finance
committees, Mr. Algeltinger accomplished
a great deal for the city, and his friends
want to see him appointed a member of
the Board of Public Works.
mRS. ERMALINH D'SYI/vTSIRA. who lived at
809 Wood street. Oakland, put an end to her ex
istence yesterday afternoon by jumping Into the
bay from the ferry boat Bay City. The. suicide
was the wife of a bÂ«arber named Manuel ,J. d'Sylvelra.
whose place of business Is on Clay street, near East, and
has been a chronic Invalid for years. It is believed that
suffering rendered her temporarily demented, as she tried
to commit suicide three months ago by taking carbolic
acid while partially out of her mind-'. .
The Bay City left the narrow gauge Oakland mote at
10 minutes after 8 o'clock and had Just passed Goat Isl
land, when a gentleman rushed Into the pilot-house and
Informed Captain Leale that two .ladies had seen a wo
man floating in the wake of the- eteamer. Captain Leale
stopped the steamer immediately, and the deckhands did
such quick work that a boat was' lowered as the steamer
came to a standstill. The boat was sent back for a dis
tance of a half mile, but the woman had ~ sunk and all
that was found was a plaid golf cape and a gray hat with
a black band and quills. On the starboard side of the
hurricane deck Captain Leale found a pair of dark tan
gloves and a purse of dark leather, the purse containing
SO cents, a ferry ticket, a scapular and a card. On one
side of the card. In script, was. the name "Mrs. Brmallne
d'Sylvelra," and on the other the address, "809 "Wood
street, Oakland. Cal."
A messenger was sent to that address, and finding no
body at home Informed Mrs. Dugrau, a near neighbor,
of what had happened. Mrs. ' Dugrau sent a drug clerk
named McCaffey to this city to Inform the husband.
D'Sylveira hastened home, and was coaxed by his friends
to go to one of their homes with his little boy Alfred, as
the. angulshr of the bereaved -husband was so great that
It was feared he would try to Join his wife in death. His
whereabouts was not divulged, but Mrs. Dugrau was per
fectly willing to state the circumstances surrounding the
suicide.
"Mrs. d'Sylveira has been an invalid for years," she
said, -'and tried to kill herself three months aero by taking
carbolic add. She took her "little boy to Oak Station to
day and sent him home with the word she was not going
to return. He told me, and I told the servant the hus
band hired to do the work some time ago. A search re
vealed that she had left a brief note to her husband that
her 6Ufterlng was more than she could bear, and she had
concluded to end It all. The two never had any domestic
trouble at all. Mrs. d'Sylvelra was 83 years old, and has
lived next door to me over 6ince she was married, at the
age of 19."

Â»h g h t ?! a 7 3 and Poems substantially
through before attempting to deal with
them analytically or to make a compre
hensive plan for finding out all there id in
them. One will not nnd Shakespeare in
a single play, although in every play
there Is something of the genius of the
ma >!h .. c m y Ht cx P lo re the entire artistic
habitation which the poet built for h!m
selr before he can feel at home with th*
architect. It counts a great deal in Judg
in S of an artist to get into the atmosphere
of his mind, and one must read many
plays before he gets into the Shakespear
ean atmosphere. It is wise, therefore, to
read the plays from beginning to end, and
to get the first and obvious delight which
comes from such reading. The man who
Is bent on analysis, either literary or phil
osophical, is not in the way of receiving
the freshest Impact from the work with
which he is dealing. To get that one xaust
surrender himself completely to the Im
pression of the work; this is what the
Shakespearean student must do In the first
Instance. He must read the plays for the
sake of the playsâ for plot, story, human
interest, character drawing and the ex
haustless delight which comes from
Shakespeare's style.
It la often assumed that because Shake
speare is a classic he is therefore unin
teresting save to cultivated people, and
Â»Â£, chlldr en especially can make little
Â°" "lF\. or saln little from him. Nothing
could* be further from the truth. To any
man who has Imagination, no matter how
defective his training, or to any chlU
who has fancy. Shakespeare Is Interest-
Ing from the very start; for the story
telling element, of which he is a master.
is as pronounced and as effective as it
is In most of the great novels. To the
uncultivated and the cultivated alike this
Is the most immediate charm of Shake
speare and therefore the easiest way of
approaching him. To regard this elemen
tal Interest as something superficial an<i
not worthy the serious student Is to miss
Shakespeare's greatest qualityâ that is to
say, his literary quality; for It is the
prime business of literature not to in
struct us, but to stimulate and enlarge
our imaginations by interesting u<?. The
book which does not Interest after a fair
approach to it may contain valuable
thoughts or solid information, but can
not be literature: because Shakespeare's
plays are primarily literature and not
ethics, philosophy or philology; they are
extremely interesting 1 . One ought not to
be afraid to read Shakespeare for the
same reason that one reads Scott, Dick
ens or Thomas Hardy.
When such an acquaintance has been
formed and the student has become fa
miliar with the plays as a whole, perhaps
the next step Is to carry to its end the
process already begun; that Is to say. to
realize clearly in the Imagination the dra
matist himself. It Is sometimes said that
it is of no consequence what kind of a
man thfe author of a piece of work or
art is, so long as the work itself Is worth
while; but the fallacy of this principle is
shown by the fact that nobody ever acts
upon it. Everybody wants to know some
thing about the man whose pictures he is
seeing, whose architecture he Is studying
or whose written words he Is reading.
This is instinctive and it la universal and
therefore there is a good reason for it.
That reason is to be found in the fact
that a man's work, if it be really great
and Individual, is part of himself. It la
not a mere expression of his skill, some
thing which he has put together as a car
penter puts a house together, or a mason
lays a wall. It is something which he
has made out of himself. The special
quality of It Is not anything which he
gains from the world without or from
other artists; It is something which he
unconsciously Imparts to It from his own
nature. Thus there Is- in the work of
?Â«Â»ry great writer something distinctive
dr<3 -Individual; compounded x>t his- -tent.*
perament, his genius and bis character.
and that something Is the most original
and precious quality in his work. Every
thing else can be Imitated or reproduced
save that elusive quality of personality.
"When people are eager, therefore, to know
something about the writer of a book
they are on the right track, although
their curiosity is sometimes more eager
than Intelligent and their way of satisfy
ing it more energetic than delicate.
There was such a man as Shakespeare,
and there are plain records of him. When
the student has felt the charm of his
work and has learned something about
It he may well try to find the man; he
has already. If he is intelligent, found a
good deal about him in his work. It In
often said that as that work is dramatic,
and therefore impersonal, there la no rev
elation of the dramatist in It. This is a
great mistake. The dramatist is not to
be found in his work in the same aense
in which the lyrical poet is generally to
be found; the sentiments which are put
upon the lips of his actors must not be
put upon his own lips; he does not reveal
himself in the direct, definite and con
crete fashion- In which the makers of
some other kinds of literature often re
veal themselves. But no one can study
the plays of Shakespeare without becom
ing aware that they disclose a general
conception of life; that they are con
structed on the theory that certain results
follow certain causes. The keynote cf
those plays Is to be found In the word
character, as the keynote of the Gree*
plays was to be found in the word des
tiny. Shakespeare believed profoundly Jn
the moral order of the world. He be
lieved also In social and political order;
he believed In order In the Individual life.
In the balance of qualities. In the subor
dination of the lower to the higher. It Is
easy to see that he was not a cynic; that
he had not an Impure mind, although the
coarseness of his time Is sometimes re
flected in his work; that he was not a
morbid man. In a word. It is not dlfflcit't
to find the spiritual man behind the work.
The order in which the plays were en
tered at the stationer's Is known. It is
therefore possible to trace the growth of
his mind. Many facts about his persona!
life are known. In spite of statements
to the contrary. Stratford Is full cf
Shakespearean associations. All this ma
terial can be found In a few good dooks
like Mr. Lee's "Life of Shakespeare. "
Professor Barrett Wendell's "William
Shakespeare." Professor Dowden's "Mind
nnd Art of Shakespeare" and Georj?
Brandes' "William Shakespeare: A Crit
ical StUdy." .en.%.
Having gotten the image of Shakespeare
in one's mind, the student will do well to
take up the plays and study them moro
critically. With the aid of a good tex*.
good notes and Introductions he ought to
understand the background of each play:
that is to say. the sources from whicli !t
was derived, the time In Shakespeare's*
life In which it was written and any pe
culiar circumstances attending either its
creation or its production.
This knowledge is not difficult of ac
cess. It Is idle for the roan who does not
intend to be a Shakesperean scholar to at
tempt to master all the Shakespearean
literature. The general student need 3 to
put himself In possession of such facts ns
will enable him to group the plays In the
order of their production with referenca
to the growth of Shakespeare's mind, to
understand the sources from which the
plots or parts of the plays were d-erived
and" the immediate historical background.
When he has done this. In addition to thÂ«s
other things which have been enumerate-1,
he Is In a position to read and enjoy the.
plays to the full. From this point he may
go rÂ»i Indefinitely to study the philosophy
of life behind the -plays, which he may
discover for himself or which he may takn
from a half-dozen authorities, among
them Denton Snider, whose very Â«uc
gestlve commentaries need not bÂ« wholly
accepted In order to be thoroughly en-
Joyed. After all. the best way to kno*
Shakespeare Is to possess a good edition
In small volumes, such an edition, for In
stance, as the Temple Shakespeare; to
carry a volume In- one's pocket; to fit
down one's newspaper reading and to de
vote one's time, as one travels In street
cars, railway trains and ferries, to the
reading of Shakespeare.

XIIL HOW TO STUDY SHAKE
SPEARE.
Ho would be a bold man rather than a
wise one who should attempt to lay down
a system of rules for the study of Shake
speare which should be equally applica
ble to persons of all conditions of intelli
gence, previous preparation and taste.
There are almost as many ways of study-
Ing as there are students; and one of the
most important results of education is
not to secure a certain amount of infor
mation or training, but to learn how to
study. The wise advlseV will not venture
in such a matter beyond a few hints,
which may be taken as suggestions rather
than as directions.
It is a matter of prime importance to
become Interested in the subject which
one is to study. When one has become
interested he may know that the mind
has taken hold, and after that the process
of perception, assimilation and digestion
goes on rapidly and almost automatically.
In the study of Shakespeare it Is best to
begin by making the poet's acquaintance
in the most direct and familiar fashion.
It is impossible to begin to study a man
Intelligently until you are fairly well ac
quainted with hfm. To study his environ
ment before you have seen him or to try
to make a chart of his characteristics be
fore he has revealed himself to you In fa
miliar intercourse Is to begin at the wrong
end and waste your time. Great works of
art are not begun by rule, nor are they
carried on by precept, although both ru'.es
and precepts may be derived from a study
of their methods after they are com
pleted. Great works of art are not mas
tered and their spiritual and artistic con
tent Is not assimilated by following ab
stract methods, although after one has
learned them by heart he may be able to
retrace and mark out the processes by
which they have been produced. A great
many useful books are written In the?e
days about other books, but these aids,
guides and commentaries ought not to be
used at the start; what Is needed then is
direct contact with the subject. The stu
dent who is going to study Shakespeare
would better begin by getting on easy
terms .with him; that Is to say, by read-
INSANE SOLDIER ESCAPES
FROM PRESIDIO HOSPITAL
ELUDED HIS GUARD WHILE BE
ING TAKEN TO BREAKFAST.
Searchers Fall to Locate VHm â Fun
eral of Mrs. Texton, "Wife of O
Troop's First Sergeant.
John Quinn, a private in Company E of
the Eighteenth Infantry and an Insane
patient In the general hospital at the Pre
sidio, escaped from his guard yesterday
morning and has not since been captured.
Qulnn came in on the Senator. He was
violent at times and so was put under
strict surveillance, a sentry being detailed
to follow him. about whenever he left his
quarters. Yesterday morning he was
taken down to breakfast In the big dln
ing-rc;gi>_ t Â« r*d In eorae-way he elude-} thÂ«
vigilance ! of hli guard and got clean
away. All yesterday men of the hospital
corps were out looking for him, but up to
late last night they had heard nothing of
him.
Qulnn is a man about 80 years of age.
When he got away he was dressed i>i
khaki trousers and a blue shirt, without
a blouse. He is clean shaven and has the
look of an Insane man in his face. It in
not considered that he is dangerous, but
he is likely to give some one a scare be
fore he is again lodged In the hospital.
His guard has gone the way of all defect
ing sentries. He has been put under ar
rest pending trial on a charge of allowing
a person in his care, similar to a prisonei ,
to escape. *
The wife of First Sergeant Texton of
Troop G of the Sixth Cavalry, who died
suddenly at the Presidio on Sunday last
of heart disease, was burled In the Na
tional Cemetery yesterday by the Order
of the Eastern Star, of which she was a
past grand matron.
Bernard McDonogh, a private of Com
pany F of the Third Infantry, died at the
general hospital yesterday. He came c\er
from the Islands on the transport Senator.
The bodies of the men brought nonw
from Manila are being shipped East very
rapidly, but they are coming In nearly as
rapidly, and so the storehouses are kept
full. The department headquarters has
had to refuse a great many applications
for bodies because they are not made by
authorized persons. The military law
takes notice of no one who is not a rela
tive or some one authorized by a relative
to arrange for the disposal of the body,
so all who are not entitled to considera
tion are being refused. Colonel Long, un
der wbom is the work of shipping the bod
ies to their proper destinations, competing
too that frequently orders regarding the
disposal of a body will come from three
or four of the same family, and they are
not always In harmony. In such cades It
takes time and a great deal of trouble to
arrive at a knowledge of Just what the
relatives wish. There has been no serious
hitch so far, however, and what delays
have occurred are chargeable more to the
relatives than to the officials in charge of
the dead.

NEW YORK. Feb. 6.â The stock market re
tained its general characteristics to-day of
strength and breadth. A large outside interest ,
was manifest and the professional operators
who bought at the lower le,vel were busy in
bidding up the prices of their holdings and
in feeding the new demand to take their
profits; but while selling stocks from the one
hand to realize on the rise they were taking
on new holding* of different stocks with the
other. Apparently the confidence that stocks
which have not yet shared In the advance
could be induced to move upward was unlm
paired and the holdings of the large financial ,
Interests which have taken hold of the market
were broadened and extended. The duration
of this kind of upward movement does not '
always depend Ftrictly on the continuance of
the public demand for stocks. There Is a con
etanl process of unloading by compact and
well organized financial Interests into the
hands of scattered and unskilled speculators
who have not the same exi*?rience and adroit
ness In protecting their holdings, ncrr the same
resources for maintaining them. The progress
of a bull movement almost inevitably results
in the transfer of holdings from strong to
weak hands and leaves the market vulnerable
to attack by bear operators who are able to
force Belling by offering down prices and un
covering margins. Even to-day attempts by
bear operators were in evidence to force de
clines, but the general demand for stocks con
tinued so good and the large holdings are still
Jn puch strong hands that the market resisted
bear assaults and presented a hard and un
yielding surface, even at points where realiz
ing was going on. Yesterday's strength In
National Steel gave way to realizing to-day
and the metal group of stocks was without
any conspicuous leadership. Norfolk and
Western was also sold to take profits all day
after an opening rise of a point, but refused
to yield below last night's level. Tennessee
Coal was forced down at one time 3% points.
Bears centered their attack on Sugar and had
that *tock down at one time to 1%4- Rumors
that the refineries were to shut down and over
prfiduction to be worked off at a loss figured
in the manipulation. But in the meantime
en aggressive buying movement developed In
the trunk lines, led by Pennsylvania, the
ooalens. led by Reading first preferred, on the
decision to pay a dividend and In the grangers
and westerns, led by St. Paul and Atchlson,
both common and preferred. The Baltimore
and Ohio also after a pause of a day to di
pest profit-taking renewed the advance. The
progress toward extension of holdings by the
(rreat railroad systems of leaser and competing
ryptems had much to do with the strength of
Pennsylvania and other lines supposed to be
In process of absorption. The coming advance
in the tidewater freight rates on bituminous
coal was also an element. In the outside mar
ket there was an advance of over 3 points to
245 In the price of Standard Oil on the declara
tion of a dividend of 20 per cent for the quar
terly period. Last year's dividends were 6,
12. I and 10 per cent respectively for the four
Quarters. There wan also activity l:i the
Amalgamated copper shares. The closing of
the n;a.rket was active and strong at about
the toj>.
The bond market showed increased activity
and general strength. Total sales, par value,
53.C25.C00.
United States 3s advanced 4 and the new 4s
U In the bid price.

New York Money Market.
NEW YORK, Feb. B.â Closeâ Money on call,
There was a larger business in securities on
the morning session, with lower quotations for
Giant Powder. Hawaiian Commercial and
Contra Costa Water, the latter declining from
$73 50 to $72 50. Pacific Gas Improvement con
tinued firm, advancing to $49 20.
The list of sales In the afternoon was longer
than for some time, though fluctuations were
neither wide nor numerous. Centra Costa
Water was still weaker at $72 25^72. and Gas
& Electric advanced at $50 374Â® 50 50.
The Caledonia Mining Company has levied
an assessment of 15 cents. Â¦
The last monthly report of the Alaska Tread
well mine., dated January 25. shows 27,092 tons
ore crushed of a value of $33,360 and 613 tons
sulphurets of a value of $14,379. Gross receipts
were $32,054 â¢ and expenses $32,173. The ore
averaged $1 95 per ton. One of the mills run
only flvft days and the stamps of both have
been hung up because of the cold weather.
Sierra Nevada Is assessed 15 cents.

London Markets.
NEW YORK, Feb. 6.â The Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
African mines opened blazing here to-day,
the market being full of rumors that Lady
smith had been relieved and that 7000 Boers,
had been captured at Colesberg. The- other
departments participated In the rise though
business was small. There was no confirma
tion of any of the rumors and prices fell away
after 2 o'clock. Consols, which were 101 H.
closed at 101. Americans maintained their
strength. The continent and arbitrage houses
bought freely though there was hardly any
London account and New York support made
the close at top prices. The chief feature was
Norfolk and Western, on the theory that that
road would be amalgamated with the Balti
more and Ohio or the Pennsylvania. Paris
was still buying Tintos and Anacondas and
higher prices for the latter were confidently
predicted.
The Bank of England bought Â£212.000 of gold
In German coin and received Â£15,000 from
Germany. For South Africa Â£21.000 was taken.
The Influence was not expected. It Is not
thought that the buying will reduce the buy
ing price of German coin to-morrow. Money
and bills were idle at unchanged rates. The
Bank of the Netherlands reduced its minimum
discount rate from 4 to 3Vi per cejit. Paris
cheque was 21.15V4; Berlin. 20.49.

Board of Trade Meeting.
At the annual meeting of the Board of Trade
Monday the following offioers were elected:
President, A. A. Watklns; first vice president.
T. J. Parsons: second vice president, S.
Fuechtwar.ger; treasurer. Panford Benr.ett: sec
retary. H. L. Pmith; attorney. Joseph Kirk.
The annual reports of the president and secre
tary show that during the twenty-three years
of the existence of the board there have been
10 IS* failures, with liabilities of JbS.S64.Bf-6, or
ar. average of S&hO per firm. The amount re
covered through the board was 137. 146. 4 M. or an
average of 53. S per cent, as against an average
of 15 per cent recovered under the legal bank
ruptcy proceedings. During 1Â«59 there were 750
cases reported to the board and 448 acted upon.
The total liabilities vrere J1.455.994, the smallest
amount in nineteen years, and the average set
tlement was 64.4 per cent. â¢

WEATHEU CONDITION'S AND GENERAL
FORECAST,
The weather is generally cloudy and threat
ening over the Pacific Slope, except In Central
and Southern California and Southern Nevada.
Light rain or snow has fallen quite generally
over the northern portion of the country.
Th* area of low pressure noted this morning
over Northern Montana lias moved southward,
causing light snow and a decided fall tn tem
nrratur; In Us wake. The pressure has ri*en
rapidly over the upper Missouri valley, and has
fallen over the southwestern portion of the
country.
The temperature has fallen decidedly over
the Rocky mountain region.
Conditions are lav. rable for c!oudy and some
what threatening weather In California and
Arizona and snow in Nevada and l"tah Wednes
day. The ten:perature will fall in ail districts.

CHICAGO. Feb. 6.â Weak, cables and heavy

In wheat at the opening. Initial tlgures for May
being He under yesterday's close, at 674<567%c.

than had been expected gradually worked the

H'S4c under yesterday at 67',ic

THE SAIST FRAXCISCO CALL,, WEDNESDAY, FEBRUARY .7, 1900.
In Sassari, Sardinia, four hundred peo-
ple are now being tried as bandits. These
include landlords, tradesmen. State and
municipal officials and many women.
There are 1900 witnesses. 120 lawyers and
200 large volumes of documents.
Phillips and His Wife.
A beautiful story is told of "Wendell
Phillips, the famous American orator. Il
lustrating his lover-like devotion to his
invalid wife, relates the New York Mail
and Express. At the close of a. lecture
engagement In a neighboring town his
friends entreated him not to return to
Boston.
It was a fearful cold night, and the last
train had gone, so he would have to re
turn In a carriage. "You will have twelve
miles of rough riding before you get
home," they said.
"But at the other end." he replied. "1
shall find Anno Phillips."
The Shepherd Canyon Road.
OAKLAND. Feb. 6.â Supervisor W.
Hemme of Contra Costa County was In
the city to-day. He said an effort will
be made to get the Contra Costa end of
the Shepherds Canyon road In a passable
condition. It has been in euch shape dur
ing the past winter that traffic has been
practically suspended.
Commercial Travelers' Club.
The Pacific Coast Travelers' Association
recently voted to admit merchants as as
sociate members, placing the fee at J5
per annum. The purpose of the move Is
to raise the necessary, money to provide
club rooms for the association, which
have long been needed by -the traveling
men as a rallying point. At the annual
meeting a vote was adopted to have club
rooms as soon as possible. The expecta
tion of Secretary A. C. Boldemann is that
the rooms will be secured by April.
Death of Mrs. Schellhaas.
OAKLAND, Feb. 6.â Mrs. Catherine
Sophie Schellhaas, mother of H. S. Schell-'
haas, the furniture dealer, is dead at the
age of 76 years. She was born in Prus
sia, and resided in Oakland for many
years: The deceased was the widow of
Caspar Echellhaas, and the mother of
Henry and Mathias Schellhaas, Mrs. F.
E. Crocker and Mrs. William Sack. The
funeral will be held to-morrow afternoon
from the. residence of Mrs. F. E. Crocker,
403 Ninth street.
Dr. J. J. Henna, a native of Puerto
Rico, now In Washington, says that there
are 258.000 beggars out of a population of
1,000,000 in that island.
A ring case has been patented by a
Michigan man for the storage of finger
rings, cleaning them automatically as
they are slipped Into place, the round
standard and screw-cap having surfaces
of abrading and polishing material which
act on the two sides of the ring as the
case Is closed. .
COUNCILMAN'S HOUSE
TACKLED BY BURGLARS
OAKLAND, Feb. 6.â An attempt was
made to rob the residence of Councilman
Glrard, â¬29 Thirty-fourth street, at an
early hour this morning. The thieves
were frustrated by the barking of a dog
which was chained In the yard.
The noise of the animal awakened the
Councilman, and when he raised a rear
window the burglars fled. An examina
tion showed that they had cut a pane of
glass out of the kitchen door and were
on the point of entering the house when
discovered. Councilman Girard now
sleeps beside a loaded shotgun. Chief of
Police Hodgklns was notified, but he has
not discovered a clew.
League of the Cross Ball.
Company A, League of the Cross Cadets
of St. Mary's Cathedral Parish, will give
an entertainment, 'to be followed by a
military ball, in Golden Gate Hall, Sutter
street, on next Friday evening. The best
amateur talent of the city .will contribute
to the programme and the grand march
will start at 9:30 o'clock. The proceeds
will be devoted to the armory and uniform
fund of the company, which has been ac
tively recruiting new members. The com
pany maintains large headquarters and
reading rooms at 614 Van Ness avenue. It
was the first company organized and has
always held a foremost place In the
League of the Cross. Tickets are 25 cents
each.

DAN MORAN'S CHARGES.
Before the Federal Grand Jury Hunt
ing Captain Schiller's Scalp for
Cruelty.
The Federal Grand Jury occupied the
whole of yesterday In examining Into
the charges preferred against Captain
John F. Schiller and William Holmes,
master-at-arms of the Government trans
port Thomas, by Daniel Moran, a coal
passer on the vessel, who alleges that he
was brutally treated on board the trans
port. The officers, on the other hand, are
trying to secure Moran's Indictment fey
mutiny. Moran'a father, on' behalf of the
boy, has entered suit against the captain
and master-at-arms for $10,000 damages.
Last Friday the Grand Jury presented
to United States District Judge de Haven
a series of questions as to whether, the
Thomas being owned and manned by the
Government and Moran being In the em
ployment of the Government, the Fed
eral courts or court-martial would be the
proper tribunals to try the Issues.
Judge de Haven called the Jury before
him yesterday and replied that the -Fed
eral Grand Jury had a right to investi
gate the charges.

DEATH OF EMINENT
NEBRASKAN PHILOSOPHER
BERKELEY. Feb. 6.â The news of the
death of Leon W. Solomons, a graduate
of the University of California, has Just
been received. Dr. Solomons was profes
sor of philosophy in the University of
Nebraska. He graduated from the Uni
versity of California in 1893 with' the de
gree fit Bachelor of Science. In 18&4 he
took his master's degree. In IS9S he re
ceived his degree or Doctor of Philos
ophy from Harvard. During the winter
<->! IS3S-i*9 Dr. Solomons was acting profes
sor of philosophy in the University of
Wisconsin and from there went to Ne
braska.*
WILLIAM HEALEY WINNER
OF AN ESSAY CONTEST
William Healey was declared winner of
the essay contest held on Monday by Com
pany D, League of the Cross Cadets, in
Sacred Heart Parish Hall. When Captain
I. D. Dwyer, who acted as . chairman,
arose to make his opening address there
was not a vacant chair in the large hall.
He stated the object of the occasion and
told what the League of the Cross was
accomplishing toward temperance and
antl-clgarette smoking.
After a number of vocal and instru
mental solos he Introduced William
Healey as the first essayist. The latter
read a particularly strong paper on the
dangers that accrue from cigarette smok
ing, and at the conclusion of his interest
ing essay he was loudly cheered. WHilaxn
Breen, another contestant, followed alonsr
the same lines. He traced the history of
the cigarette and wound up with a dia
tribe against Its use by young men.
The judges, T. J. Walsh and Miss
Dunne, then retired, and returned a few
minutes later and rendered a decision in
favor of Mr. Healey, and he is now enti
tled to compete in the annual essay con
test of the organization. Rev. Father La
gan delivered a short address, and after
the national hymn was sung by the audi
ence the meeting closed.
NARROW ESCAPE FROM
DEATH BY SUFFOCATION
, ALAMEDA, Feb. 6.â Several persons in
the residence of George *W. Scott, 1723
Central avenue, were overcome by smoke
during a fire there at an early hour this
morning and narrowly escaped death.
Mrs. H. F. Whitman and Miss Maud Scott
were carried from the burning structure
in an unconscious condition by the fire
men, and it took a considerable time to
resuscitate them.
The nre originated from a defective
furnace in the basement and was unaer
considerable headway when the depart
ment arrived. Chief Krauth and several
of his men made several ineffectual at
tempts to enter the house before they
were successful. The smoke was den>e
and drove them from the place repeat
edly. A ladder was finally placed against
an attic window and the inmates were
rescued by means of it. Exit had been
cut off in other quarters.
The flames were confined to the lower
portion of the structure, but considerable
damage was done by water and the loss
will reach several thousand dollars.
Besides the two women who were car
ried from the house in an unconscious
condition, the hcuse contained H. F. Whit
man, four children and two servantts.
They were all considerably overcome by
the smoke but managed to climb down
the ladder unaided. --.'â¢'
Mr. and Mrs. Scott are at present In
New York City on a visit.
