MISS MARTHA ALEXANDER
WEDS JOHN WATERHOU SE
MRS. JOHN WATERHOUSE (Nee ALEXANDER).
nAKLAND, Feb. 6.â The wed
ding .of Miss Martha Alexan
\V^ der, youngest daughter of Mr.
and Mrs. S. T. Alexander, and
John Waterhouse of Honolulu took
place this evening at 8 o'clock at the
handsome residence of the bride's pa
rents, corner Sixteenth and Filbert
streets. The ceremony, which was
performed by the Rev. Charles R.
Brown of the First Congregational
Church, was witnessed by only the
relatives and a few very Intimate
friends. A large reception was held
Immediately following the service.
The Alexander home was most
beautifully decorated for the event.
The wide hall was arranged with cut
flowers and palms and ferns and clus
ters of red roses were placed here
and there. The reception room was
entirely In green, smilax being used.
The drawing and dining rooms werp
In white and lavender. Chinese lan
terns lighted the canvas-covered steps
and portico.
The ceremony was very simple, but
impressive. The bride, who Is petite
and Blender, was charming in her
gown of white satin. She wore a veil
and carried lilies of the valley. Her
maid of honor, Miss Harriett Kitt
redge, wore an elegant gown of white
with an overdress of point d'esprit.
She carried white blossoms. The
bridesmaids were Miss Belle Dickie,
a cousin, and the Misses Carmen
Moore and Emma Grimwood. They
were dressed alike in white liberty
silk. Wallace Alexander, the bride's
brother, was best man. The recep
tion that followed the ceremony was
attended by several hundred guests,
including some of the most prominent
society people on both sides of the
bay.
The bride is a member of Oakland's
younger set, and has been very prom
inent at the different cotillons and
other smart affairs held this season.
She is extremely popular because of
her genial and sweet disposition. Mr.
Waterhouse is prominent in the isl
ands, where the Alexanders have ex
tensive interests and where they
spend a part of each year. The two
families have been intimately ac
quainted for years and the union Is
one that Is particularly pleasing. The
young couple were the recipients of
many elegant gifts, chief among
which was the deed of an elegant
home in Honolulu, a present from the
bride's father.
DEVISED ALL TO
HER WHO WAS A
MOTHER TO HIM
KNOCKED FROM
A BOX CAR AND
TORN IN SHREDS
W. F. Montres In
stantly Killed.
s Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1118 Broadway, Feb. 6.
W. F. Montrefe, a switchman, was
knocked from the top of a boxcar by a
smokestack which projected over the
track from the trainshed at the Alameda
mole this morning. He was instantly
killed. Four wheels of a car passed over
his body, mangling it In a horrible man
ner.
The accident occurred Just after 4
o'clock. Montres was standing on top of
the car, which was being shunted by a
yard engine, and he failed to see the ob
struction. When struck .the switchman
fell between the cars and across the
track. Some of the wheels passed over
the body, almost cutting it in two. An
other wheel passed over the head of the
unfortunate man, crushing it to a pulp.
The body was taken to the Morgue and
Coroner Mehrmann will hold an Inquest.
The deceased was a yard switchman and
had been In the employ of the railroad
company for several years. He was con
sidered a careful and responsible man.
Montres was about 28 years of age and
unmarried. He resided at the Bay View
House near the mole, and has parents
living at Boulder Creek, Santa Cruz
County. Â¦
Mrs. Lanktree Files
W. P. Jones.W ill.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1118 Broadway, Feb. 6.
Because she had cared for and guarded
over him as a loving mother for more than
twenty-one years William P. Jones, the
aged capitalist and bachelor, who died in
Alameda last week, left his entire estate
to Mrs. Susan Lanktree.
Mr. Jones was 78 years old at the time
of his death, January 29 last. His will,
executed December 19, 5899, was filed for
probate this afternoon, and Mrs. Susan
Lanktree, who is named as sole devisee
and also executrix without bonds, has ap
plied, for letters of administration. The
instrument is very brief. It simply recites
that "I devise and bequeath all my es
tate, real, personal or mixed, wheresoever
situated, including all property of which
I may die seized, possessed or In any way
entitled to or interested In, to my friend,
Susan Lanktree. now residing at my home
in Alameda." The clause making her ex
ecutrix follows.
Mrs, Lanktree's petition for letters re
cites that the estate is valued at about
J30.500, being $12,500 of real estate In Ala
meda and Oakland and $18,300 of personal
property.
Jones came to California across the
plains in 1849. He never married, and so
far as can be learned left no relatives or
heirs at law.. For over twenty-one years
Mrs. Lanktree had guarded over him
with almost the same tenderness as she
exerted over her son. J. B. Lanktree.
LAW'S METHODS
CRITICIZED BY
A LADY LAWYER
Clara Foltz Creates a
Scene in Court.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
HIS Broadway. Feb. 6.
"Do you expect the women of this Stat*
to take the law in their own hands, your
Honor? This man Freeman ought to bo
sent to jail!"
After addressing these stinging worrla
to Superior Judge Hall in open court to
day, Clara Foltz, the woman . attorney,
representing Mrs. Sarah Freeman, Â»ho
had her husband cited for contempt in
refusing to pay alimony, rushed from lli-j
courtroom, followed by her fair client an I
several lady friends, and a3 she hurried
through the corridor she waa heard to
murmur something about "a moral cow
ard, that's all."
Judge Hall had just dismissed the cita
tion directing Thomas B. Freeman to ex
plain why he should not be punished for
â¢having failed to pay his divorced vife
*534 back alimony"; why he had. as his ex
wife alleged in her petition, â¢'defle.l the
authority of the courts" and "sneered at
the majesty of the law."
On twenty previous occasions Freeman
had been cited for similar tardiness- in
obej-lng the court's decree. On each oc
casion he had the sum total due Increase
J2O. And each time Freeman had escaped
without a touch, claiming that he had
been haled to court so frequently that
he could not discover an opportunity to
earn enough to meet the demands of the
law. On the witness stand Freeman de
nied that he defied the court's authority
or sneered at the majestic law. "I. have
no reason for so doing." he said. "The
courts have always treated me fairly."
"And do you think," queried Mrs. Foltz.
plaintiff's attorney, "that because the
court has stood by you twenty tlm^s thÂ»
court ought to make it twenty-one?"
Judge Hall mildly reproved the fairer
Bexed advocate and prevented Freeman's
reply by admonishing her that "your re
marks are very offensive to this court ."*
and Mrs. Foltz apologized profusely,
while his Honor dismissed the citation.
Judge Hall gave the plaintiff but one
consolation. W. B. Woodward, Freeman's
employer, had testified that the defend
ant had worked only part of the time
since the last citation and had not eamc<l
enough to pay the alimony, and Judge
Hall remarked that If ever Freeman do? 3
earn or accumulate enough he can be
brought Into court and be made to pay.
An effort on the part of Mrs. Foltz to
have the citation go over for a month
was knocked early in the game.
SCHOOLMARMS
MUST CEASE
USING SLANG
Trustees Condemn
Nicknames.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
HIS Broadway, Feb. 6.
Teachers who persist in calling pupils
by nicknames hereafter are liable to of
ficial decapitation. Tne practice has been
prohibited by an edict of the Board of
Education. * The slang of the street must
be expurgated from the pedagogic vocab
ulary, and nothing but the purest of
Queen's English Is to be hereafter toler
uted. Curbstone language Is alleged to
have been used by demure and pretty
young schoolma'ams in addressing chil
dren of unruly disposition and, further
more, It is asserted that some of the ob
streperous kids were dubbed with derisive
noms de plume.
The matter was brought before the
Board of Education last night. All the
members were a unit in declaring that
hereafter a teacher guilty of such an act
shall be liable to a peremptory dismissal
from service. The particular case which
brought the practice up for attention was
one wherein a boy was contemptuously
called "Tougny" by an irate teacher.
Superintendent McClymonds was told to
make an investigation, and should he find
that the use of nicknames by teachers la
prevalent it will be stopped, even though
the official guillotine has to be brought
into requisition.
"Here, Swipsey, come up here and put
on the duncecap" will no longer be ut
tered by an angry young miss In address
ing a pupil.
"Shorty, "Fatty." "Red." "Blinky,"
"The Kid," "Three-Fingered Jack."
"Rain-In-the-Face." "The Spider," "Run
away Bill." "Petey." "Brlcktop." "Gib
lets." "Nibs" and the rest of the crowd
of 10-year-olds with eimila/ descriptive
appellations have been tabooed. Teachers
have been officially notified arid ordered
to erase them from the books of choice
names of pet pupils.
"John Henry," "James Augustus," "Al
fonso," "Clarence" or whatever other
name a budding genius may have hap
pened to have- been cumbered with at the
baptismal font must now be used, t when
the boys are addressed by their Instruc
tors. Opprobrious phrases are also ta
booed.
"I think that the cases where teachers
use nicknames or slang In reproaching a
pupil are isolated ones," said Assistant
Superintendent of Schools R. B. S. York.
"Even if that Is the case, however, it
must be stopped altogether. Superintend
ent McClymonds has started in to make
a thorough investigation, and if any of
the teachers are. found guilty of such
conduct tney will be suspended or ex
pelled from duty. Children are sensitive,
and when contemptuously referred to by
their instructors they feel it keenly, and
it makes them sullen and rebellious.
Kindness, firmness and politeness are the
three best things to govern pupils with
in a school. If a child persists in being
refractory after being handled in that
manner, the case should be referred to
the Superintendent and he will dispose
of it."
Many of the teachers are shocked to
learn that charges of such indecorous be
havior have been made against some of
their number. They all assert most posi
tively that such cases are rare, If they
exist at all.
SHE WILL WED
HIM EVEN IN
HIS OWN HOME
Miss Darneal True to
Alan Bowen.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
HIS Broadway, Feb. 6.
Alan Bowen and his approaching wed
ding to Miss Susie Darneal of Alameda
have caused a rupture in the Darneal
household to such an extent that the ap
proaching wedding has been announced
for the home of the groom, and there ia
consternation where this fact is known.
The groom is giving his opinion of his
mother-in-law-elect before marriage and
the mother-in-law-elect has a few words
to say. about the new addition to the
household, which words are supplemented
by some from the father. Meanwhile Miss
Darneal says tnat she is going to marry
Mr. Bowen, even if she has to do It at the
home of his parents.
Alan Bowen is the son of Charles G.
Bowen of the firm of Wetmore, Bowen &
Co., the San Francisco wine merchants.
Miss Susie Darneal is the- daughter of
Harvey Darneal, one of the court report
ers of this county. The parents of the
young lady knew nothing about the ap
proaching wedding until it was announced
and they do not hesitate to oppose it
openly.
"If my daughter leaves her parents for
this man she leaves our house forever,"
said Mr. Darneal to-day. "Of course she
is of age and can do as she pleases, but
it will not be with our consent. She can
not be married in our home and none of
our family will be present. We have only
one cause of objection to this young man
â we do not think he is a proper one for
our daughter to marry. We will offer no
objection, but we will not assist in the
wedding."
This, is what Mr. Bowen says in reply:
"The statement that I have been denied
the privilege of visiting Mls3 Darneal at
her home is not quite correct. I call there
frequently and am received as courteously
as I could expect in view of the fact that
her parents object to mo as a son-in-law.
The opposition comes principally from
Mrs. Darneal. She has brought every
possible argument to bear against our
marriage. She has not succeeded in her
efforts, however, and the ceremony will
take place as planned. I consider myself
quite the equal of Mrs. Darneal so far as
lineage goes, for two of my ancestors
fought in the Revolutionary War. I am
a Junior member of the firm of Wetmore.
Bowen & Co. of San Francisco. The fact
that the firm deals in liquors is one of the
arguments used against me."
Miss Darneal is true to her accepted
lover and she declares her intention of
marrying him. despite her parents.
"Our wedding day has been set and
that's all there is to it." said Miss Dar
neal. "I have known Mr. Bowen for a
long time and have learned to regard him
highly. The antagonism of my parents to
our approaching marriage is without good
reason. At present we are agreed that
the marriage shall take place on the 19th
at Mr. Bowen's home. If my parents re
lenj.. then we will be married at my own
home. There will be nothing elaborate
about the affair.
And so Alameda 13 all "agog.
WHEELER IS NOT
GOING EAST FOR
NEW MATERIAL
At Least Not on This
Little Trip.
BERKELEY. Feb. 6.â President Benja
min Ide Wheeler* of the University of Cal
ifornia left for the East last night, to be
gene for several weeks. His mission is
I not. he says, that of seeking new material
| for the university, but one of obligation.
While a professor at Cornell University
he was engaged to give a series of five
lectun-s on "Greek Life" to the students
of the university at Richmond, Va. In
addition he will speak at the dedicatory
exercises of the Perkins Men's building
at Colorado Springs, at the Washington's
birthday celebration of JohnsllopkinsUni
versity, before the Civic League of Chi
cago and at the national convention of
Superintendents of Education.
But while the president will not admit
to more than a lecturing tour upon this
trip he says that he is going East again
and will then fish out some new material
for the university faculty. In view of thlt
admission a great deal of wonder Is being
expressed in Berkeley as to whether
President Wheeler will not keep a quiet
lookout on this trip for men who might
wain to help ljuild the greater University
of California. There have been many ru
mors as to what President Wheeler in
tended to do with his staff/and it is of
interest to know what the new president
thinks. Before leaving for the East he
made this statement:
"So, I am not going East to get new
material for the University of California,
I will make a special trip a little later for
that purpose. The time is not ripe for any
changes if there be any intention of mak
ing changes. My trip will be well taken
up with official visits and lectures in vari
ous States. If the time were opportune
and everything else agreeable I could not
possibly do Justice to such a matter in the
limited time at my disposal.
"i do not know of any changes in the
faculty that are contemplated. The uni
versity is growing and changes and addi
tions must necessarily be made. At pres
ent, however, nothing can be done be
cause of the lack of funds. That is the
one thing which will prevent the Univer
sity of California from going to the front.
As soon as we can get appropriations
from the State or money from private
sources, then the college will be put on a
new footing.
"Since I came here I have made a care
ful etudy of the needs of the university
aa regards the chairs and equipment. The
equipment in some departments needs ad
ditions and I trust will be straightened
out before another year. For the past few
weeks I have visited the recitations that
I might be familiar with every professor
and instructor and every department. If
we see our way clear a little later in the
year I will go East again and devote my
entire time to getting men who will fit the
positions they are chosen for in the best
possible manner. We want the best men
we can secure, and when we have that
the future of the university is assured.
If the State will do its part there need be
no fear for the future of the University
of California."

6 Henry to 2WThe Mysells Absorb Another Large
Block of Stock of the Mysell-
Rollins Co.
Win. C. Mysell was elected president and
general manager of the Mysell-Rolllns Co.
at its annual meeting. Among other
changes a large holding of the Rollins in
terest changed hands. The Mysells have
absorbed a large block of the stock to the
extent of about $80,000. which carries with
it the franchise holding of the American
Bank Stock Co., its watermarks and
patents.

OUTBREAK OF
HIGHBINDER
WAR AT HAND
Police Taking Many
CHINESE SIX COMPANIES ACT
RIGID ENFORCEMENT OF THE
LAW BY THE ACTING CHIEF.
The police are preparing for another
outbreak of the highbinder war in China
town. Several things have transpired
within the last few days which have given
rise to the conclusion that murder is agaJn
on the tapis and that the truce had sim
ply been declared for the purpose of hood
winking the police into granting unlimited
privileges to the Mongolians during their
new year celebration. So serious have
matters trended during the past few days
that a meeting of the Chinese Six Com
panies was held last night to raise money
with which to compensate the Hop Sing
tong for the lives they lost during the
past portion of the war and to offer an
inducement to them to keep the peace.
Detective McMahon stated last night
that a murder might be expected on any
day. He says that the undercurrent of
fear among the Chinese, which charac
terized the first part of the struggle, has
again manifested itself, and the highbind
ers have stowed themselves away, in
places of seclusion, from which they
emerged to participate in the New Year
festivities. The police were obliged to
liberate a highbinder recently whose iden
tity as a murderer had been established,
because Chinese witnesses on whose testi
mony they relied had been seized with the
war fever arid escaped.
Last night the members of the Chinese
Six Companies held a meeting at their
headquarters for the purpose of devising
means by which to ward off the trouble
which seems Imminent. It will be remem
bered that no money .was offered to the
worsted tong for lives that had been sac
rificed from its ranks, and this fact alone
made all "who are acquainted with Chinese
affairs look with suspicion on the peace
compact. The Six Companies took the
matter of the compact under advisement
and sought to make it binding by having
a clause offering an amount of money for
the lives lost inserted in it. Representa
tives of the Hop Sing tong who were
present winked their eyes and refused to
accept any other declaration than that
already signed by them. The elimination
of a monetary consideration is said to be
sufficient excuse for a renewal of hostili
ties, with revenge as an object.
Acting Chief Biggy recognizes the se
rious situation and has taken precautions
which have never been taken before. As
a result Chinatown is more firmly under
police control than at any time in its past
history. Everything that contributes to
the highbinders' coffers has been sup
pressed, and this method iÂ« rtgarded as
the only one instrumental in rooting out
bold crime. All the gambling- houses in
that section of the city have been closed
and the paid assassins have been hounded
from their holes. Police constantly patrcl
the streets for the purpose of enforcing
a strict observance of the laws, and only
competent officers have been chosen for
such duty. -

J|iSg|flMA. OFFICE,
Death of Miss E. R. C. Sargent.
Friends of Miss Elizabeth R. C. Sargent
will learn with sorrow of the death of that
estimable lady. She passed away in this
city yesterday after a short illness. She
was a daughter of the late Senator Aaron
A. Sargent and Ellen O. Sargent. Miss
Sargent was well known in society circles
of Nevada City. Washington. D. C.and
Newburyport. Mass.

Thorlow Â¦ McMullln's stilt for divorce
against his wife, Virginia McMullln. was
again taken up by Judge Bahrs yester
day. Mr. McMullln occupied the stand
during' the day. but nothing of interest
was developed. Annie L. Reidy was
granted a divorce from Henry Reidy on
the ground of desertion. Nellie Anderson
has sued J. M. Anderson for a divorce,
alleging cruelty, as cause of action.

MRS. DAVALOS' DEATH.
Dr. Thomas M. Lleb, Kearny and Clay
streets, was arrested yesterday afternjon
by Detectives Crockett and Graham anJ
locked up in "the tanks" pending the re
sult of the Coroner's inquest on the body
of Mrs. Manuela Davalos, 1904 Mason
street. The woman died from the result
of a criminal operation alleged to hive
been performed by Dr. Lieb. Her hus
band is employed on the steamer Austra
lia, and before her death she acknowl
edged to him that Dr. Lieb had performed
the operation. She was" the mother of
four children.

POLICE BOARD MEETS.
No Attempt Will Be Made to Elect a
Chief Until Commissioner
Thomas Returns.
President Newhall upon convening the
Police Commissioners last night an
nounced that owing to the absence of
Commissioner Thomas the board had de
cided to take no action regarding the elec
tion of Chief of Police, preferring to wait
until all the Commissioners could be pres
ent.
The remaining three hours of the ses
sion were mostly. taken up with the con
federation of applications for liquor li
censes. Four hundred and forty-six appli
cations, reported upon favorably by the
nergeants and patrolmen to whom they
had been referred for investigation, were
gTanted. Applications rejected at the lest
meeting by reason of the fact that ap
plicants had rooms connected with their
saloon* with partitions higher than al
lowed by the ordinance were reconsidered
and granted upon the understanding. that
applicants would reconstruct these rooms
to conform to the requirements.
An application by Charles Mitche'.l for
a saloon license on Dunbar alley brought
the statement from Commissioner New
hall that there were altogether too many
saloon? In the vicinity of the Hall of Jus
tice. Personally, he said, he Could not see
the necessity for more. Action on the ap
plication was deferred for a week.
Keepers of dance halls on Grant avenue,
between Market and Sutter streets, were
Informed by the Commissioners that -ft
was the object of the board to clear that
thoroughfare of euch places. Licenses
irerc granted with the undemanding that

WODED HER
WITH A BIG
REVOLVER
Widow Beady Has a
Thrilling Escape.
J. L. Matkovich, who has charge of the
lunch counter at the Good Fellows' Grot
to, was arrested last evening by Police
men Perry and Barry and booked at the
City Prison on a charge of carrying a
concealed weapon. That the charge is
not one of a more serious nature Matko
vich has the oillcers to thank.
Matkovich has been courting Mrs
Beudy. a rather prepossessing widow,
who resides at 320 Van Ness avenue, and
becoming jealous of the attentions W C
Brown was paying the object of his'af
fections the dispenser 'Of cold roast boet
and ham sandwiches took aboard an un
usually large quantity of liquor last night
and paid a hostile visit to the widow's
house.
There he collided with Mrs. Beady 1 sÂ»
brother, who rather favors Brown for
a brother-in-law, and Matkovich, who was
in a beautifully mellow state of intoxica
tion, proceeded to make a rought house
out of the little home. Mrs
Beady 1 and her brother joined forces and
the combination was so effectual that
Matkovich reached down" into his hin
pocket and drew fortn a glistening re
volver. Mrs. Boady and her brother were
undaunted by Matkovich's warlike actions
and in another instant he received a cuff
on the ear from the. angry brother that
sont him sprawling on the floor, where
Mrs.. Beady sat upon him until the polices
arrived. Â¦ . . ..â¢Â¦:, .:Â¦ Â¦â¢ .Â»â¢.,.:â¢>.

SERVICE LIST ADOPTED
Various Places of Employment Seg
regated by the Commission â First
Examination March 1.

Service Commission adopted the classified
civil service under which examinations
will be held for positions in those depart
ments of the city government under civil
service Jurisdiction. These Include the
offices of the County Clerk. Assessor. Tax
Collector, Sheriff. Auditor. Recorder,
Coroner. Board Â©f Public Works. Police
and Fire departments. Board of Election
Commissioners. Board of Health, the
clerks and stenographers of the Justices
and Police courts and all boards or de
partments controlling public utilities. ThÂ©
places of employment have been segre
gated Intofceven classes, as follows: Cler
fcal. mechanical, custodian, engineering,
medical, police and fire. The classification
is as follows:
CLERICAL.
(1) Copyists. (2) stenographers and typewrit
ers (3) ordinary clerks. (4) bookkeepers, (a) ex
perienced clerks. (6) register clerks. (7) court
room clerks, (8) office deputies. (9) secretaries.
MECHANICAL.

men and pavers, (4) tinners. (5) plumbers. (8)
plasterers (7) painters. (8) masons. (9) ma
chinists. (10) cabinet-makers. (11) carpenters.

ENGINEERING.

graph operators. (10) steam engineers, (11) elec
trical engineers. (12) civil engineers.
MEDICAL.
(1) Health officers. (2) sanitary Inspectors. (3)
hospital employes. (4) Â«â¢ bu >a nce . a "?'Vl lll "2:

MAY APPOINT A
WOMAN AS ONE
OF HIS STAFF
Perplexity of School
Superintendent.
CANNOT GET MEN TO SERVE
SELECTION OF A. L. JORDAN THE
ONLY CEBTAINTY.
Superintendent of Schools Webster has
found it such a difficult matter to per
suade members of the department to ac
cept positions of "assistant deputies under
him at the salary of $150 per month, as
fixed by the Board, of Education, thut
yesterday he expressed his opinion that
he would be obliged to name a woman
for one of the places. In fact, he says
he has several names under consideration,
but had not finally made up his mind
whether the necessity for appointing a
woman was unavoidable or not. He saya
further that he had already received sev
eral protests against any possible Inten
tion he may have had to appoint a lady
and, strange to relate, the protests do not
come from the sterner sex. The fact that
a deputy superintendent must report on
the qualifications of the teachers and the
conditions of the classes as he or she
may find them makes it peculiar that the
ladies are averse to one of their own sex
performing the duties. . .
The only name Mr. Webster has posi
tively decided upon for one of the places
Is that of A. L. Jordan, who is now his
principal assistant. The other three dep
uties have not as yet been decided on.
Mr. Webster finds that- the low salary
and the ten years' practical school-ex
perience required -by the charter for the
position have limited his scope of availa
ble tandldates. Men who have the neces
sary experience decline to give up the
higher salaries they are now earning to
work at duties considerably more ardu
ous that they are at present performing.
Director Casserly's recent suggestion that
."young men- be given a chance to grow
up with the work" does not find much
favor with Webster, who thinks that Cas
serly is evidently not. familiar. with the
ten-year requirement In the charter,
which effectually bars the said young
It is believed nothing, would please the
board better than to appoint Mr. Web
ster's deputies, providing he consents,
which is quite likely if he cannot get the
men he wants to serve at the $150 stipend.
In fact, it is said the board placed the
salary at that figure with the sincere ex
pectation that it would be impossible to
get desirable men who would serve at
that price. If the board be allowed to
appoint the deputies, it is more than like
ly the salary may be raised to $200 or
even $250 a month, a figure which Su
perintendent Webster and Chairman
Mark both agree Is Just and proper.
THE grave closed over the body
of betrayed and deserted Amy
Murphy yesterday forenoon, and
before the sun had set the prison's
steel clasp was upon the llvinc:
form of Horace Poulin. the man wh<>
chewed her the way where lay shame and
a miserable death.
Though he has been brought to know
the interior of a prison cp!l it Is not
likely that the courts of earth can punish
this man. but when Poulin goes into the
world again it will be as a wanderer from
the city that has been his home for so
long. Discredited by those who have
known him. discharged by the firm in
â¢whose employ he had been so long, and
unable to make new connections because
of the path of degradation he has chosen
to follow. Horace Poulin will leave San
Francisco to try life in some place where
the darker fide of his character has not
been ghown to the public gaze.
The Incident of Amy Murphy's passing
out of the world is now practically closed.
Whether she committed suicide or was
murdered may still be a question in the
minds of some who have followed the
case; but the only thing now, after the
clods hov#> fallen on her coffin, that can
revive public Interest in the case would be
the accidental turning up of the pistol or
the locating of the man who reported the
discovery of the body. Either of these hap
penings would probably only result in the
further substantiation of the suicide
theory-
Acting Chief of Police Biggy. though re
alizing that there was no law under
which Poulin could be punished for the
killing of Miss Murphy, was determined
that if it lay in his power Poulin should
be punished in some way for the wrongs
done her.
Actuated by this determination Mr.
Biggv yesterday afternoon had a tele
phone message sent to Poulin at the office
of the Eisen Vineyard Company asking
him to come to headquarters. Poulin hap
pened to be there, and soon after 5
o'c.ock he appeared at the station. Un
der an order from the 'Acting Chief he
was taken to the City Prison. He was
fcooked on a charge of "adultery." in or
der that he might bÂ« held until the law
could be looked up and a determination
reached as to whether he could be prose
cuted under any of the statutes for his
offenses against morality. The Acting
Chief himself plunged into the statute
books to find something to cover the case,
If possible-
Acting Chief Biggy was very angry
Â¦when he lu-ard that Poulin had been
booked at the prison for "adultery." He
had had him taken there merely to be
held until the law could be looked up.
Under the statute In cases euch as these
an adultery charge will not lie unless at
least one of the parties Is married.
Poulin was placed in one of the large
barred compartments where petty offend
ers are confined. He soon struck up a
conversation with one of the several in
the "cage" with him and seemed much
Interested. :>*-<
"Say. give me time to collect my
thpughts, won't you?" he said when
tsked in the jail office about matters con
nected with his case. "This is a new
thing for me. I never was in a jail be
fore. Of course I know you fellows
haven't any sentiment In matters of this
kind. It if=n't your business, but my
head's Bwimming. Don't crowd me too
much. You know I've got a few things
on my mind."
Poulin walked about nervously while
saying this. There was a weak but not a
forced smile on his lips, his face was pale
end he pulled between words at a cigar
ette. He Is of slender build and has a

THE LATE MRS. JAMES PHELAN.
mRS. JAMES PHELAN, mother of the Mayor, died at the family res
idence on Valencia street yesterday mornir.g. Since last Thanksgiv
ing day her life has been despaired of, and it was- feared that her
two daughters, who were in the East, might not again see her alive,
but for several weeks the patient was sustained, and when the end came she
was surrounded by all her children. One week ago it seemed as though Mrs.
Phelan was about to regain some of her former strength, but Monday morning
she suffered a third relapse, and her physicians at once declared that there
was no hope of another rally.
Mrs. Phelan was a descendant of one of the most historic families of the
. ancient Irish nobility. She was formerly Miss Alice Kelly, daughter of the
late Jeremiah Kelly of Stradbally, Queens County, Ireland. The family de
scended from the O'Mores of Leix, which line was almost exterminated at the
fearful massacre of Mullagast. Among the treasured relics in the Phelan
family is a cross of St. Louis, bestowed on the O'Mores for bravery during
the French wars, and which descended as an heirloom to Mrs. Phelan.
The Kelly family came to this country over half a century ago and settled
in Brooklyn, N. V., where Mrs. Duval, a sister of the deceased, still lives.
It was in New York that Miss Alice Kelly met James Phelan, her future hus
band, who went West to lay the foundation of the vast fortune which he after
ward accumulated. In ISSB the marriage should have been performed, but
when James Phelan was about to leave California to go East and claim his
bride his brother Michael died and delayed the journey. The next year, how
ever. Miss Kelly, accompanied by General Joseph Lane and Hon. Lafayette
Grover of Oregon, came to this coast, and in May, ISSD, she. became the, wife
of James Phelan, the ceremony being performed by Archbishop Alemany in
old St. Mary's Cathedral.
With the exception of a few trips to her old home Mrs. Phelan has re
sided continuously in this city since her marriage, and all of her children were
born here. Nine years ago her husband died, and by the terms of his will
Mrs. Phelan was given a life interest in the city and country establishments
o? the family and an annual Income of $30,000. Her unpretentious mode of liv
ing did not require more than a small proportion of this magnificent income,
and the larger part has been used to aid the. less fortunate. It has been dis
pensed In such a manner, however, that the right hand knew not the mission
of the left. Mrs. Phelan was very earnest in her observance of religious
duties, and so long as she was able she was regularly in her place at St.
Ignatius Church. AY*
Mrs. Phelan was born 73 years ago. Her children are James D. Phelan,
Miss Mollle Phelan and Mrs. Frank J. Sullivan.
The funeral will take place to-morrow at 10:30 o'clock from St. Ignatius
Church, and the ceremonies over the grave, which will be at Holy Cross
Cemetery, will be attended only by relatives and friends..
LIVELY SCENES
WHILE LOADING
THE AUSTRALIA
Freight Blockades on
East Street.
HONOLULU LACKS PROVISIONS
A BIG LOAD WILL LEAVE ON THE
MAIL STEAMEB.
East street from Market to the Oceanic
dock was crowded with heavy laden wag
ons from early morning yesterday until
noon to-day. Teamsters cursed and
swore, pedestrians crossed the thorough
fare at their peril, electric cars were
blockaded and there were several pitched
battles between the drivers of the horse
cars and the drivers of the wagons.
Through it all the horses enjoyed a well
earned rest and as they got their three
square meals throughout the day they
doubtless would not object if the blockade
lasted for a week.
The Oceanic Steamship Company's mail
boat Australia is to sail for Honolulu to
day, at 2 p. m. Little or no freight has
reached the islands for over a month and
in consequence almost all articles of food
are at starvation prices. Potatoes are a
luxury in the Paradise of the Pacific at
the present time. When the Australia
was there a small sized tuber was worth
5 cents, and the chances are they have
doubled in value since then. This is only
an Instance, as nearly all the staple arti
cles of daily use are very scarce.
No wonder tuen that when the Aus
tralia began to receive freight there was
a rush of gooda. At 8 a. m. it was im
possible to get within a block of the
wharf and Sergeant "Tom" Mahoney. and
a squad of police had a hard time pre
serving the peace. At 10 a. m. they nad
got the line straightened out so that out
side teams could come and go, but at 3
p. m. the heavily laden wagons were still
three deep from the Oceanic dock to Mar
ket street. On the wharf Captain How
ard had everything in splendid working
order. There was no confusion and the
loading of the mall steamer went on with
a rush and wknout a hitch.
There' is almost twice as much freight
offering for Honolulu as the Australia
can carry and the chances are that the
Zealandia will be put on the run. She is
now fitted out and ready for the Cape
Nome trade in the spring, but she can
easily mai.e a trip to Hawaii before the
rush to the gold fields begins.
The transports are beginning to move
again. The Conemaugh will begin load
ing horses to-day and will sail for Ma
nila via Hilo to-morrow. She will be fol
lowed by thr> Leelanaw in a" couple of
days. The Meade will load freight and
will probably get away about the 18th
inst. The Thomas came off the dock'
yesterday and will follow the Meade on
the berth. The Board of Examiners met
yesterday and discharged the Manauense
The report of the Board of Inquiry, which
has taken evidence In the matter of the
aUeped unseaworthiness of the vessel, will
be ready in a few days.
The British ship Clackmannanshlre Is
once more on her way to Europe with a
cargo of wheat. While making her way
down the Columbia River she was In col
lision and the fluke of her anchor was
driven through one of the after bow
plates. The cargo in the forepeak was
removed, the damage repaired and now
the ship that ia named after the smallest
county in Scotland is once more at sea.
The. tug Annie lost her smokestack
through the carelessness of the crew of
the transport Thomas early yesterday
morninr. The Thomas came down from
the drydock during the night and berthed
along the end of Howara -street wharf.
.To keep her In position, a line was run
ahead to Mission street wharf. The An
nie came along in the dark and Captain
Fitchworth proceeded to enter the slip,
never dreaming there was a line across
It. In an Instant the Annie's smokestack
was gone and the tug is now laid up for
repairs.
The Commissioner of Navigation has
authorized the change of the name of the
steamer Ira waddy to that -of Charles D.
Lane. The steamer is owned by the Wild
Goose Mining and Trading Company, of
which Charles D. Lane is an owner.
